


2004 WL 1167174 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HARRIS KRISTANTO SARENGAT NG
File: A78 611 715 - Memphis
February 9, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Camille O. Bowman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Scott D. Gordon
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider our earlier denial of a motion to reopen on October 20, 2003. The motion, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service)1, will be denied.
 
In our October 20, 2003, decision we considered a motion claiming ineffective assistance of the respondent’s former attorney. We determined that the motion to reopen, having been filed almost 10 months after our final order of August 28, 2002, had been filed out of time. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
Despite the respondent’s arguments in the motion to reconsider, we still find no adequate excuse for the lateness of the respondent’s motion to reopen. As we stated in our earlier decision, our August 28, 2002, decision was promptly received by the respondent (Respondent’s Affidavit to Motion to Reopen). When the respondent consulted an individual he considered to be his attorney about the Board’s decision, the attorney said he was unable to assist him. Id. The respondent then waited to hire new counsel for 9 months. Id. Certain courts have determined that time and numerical limitations on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled. See Scorteanu v. INS, 339 F.3d 407, 413 (6th Cir.2003). However, equitable tolling is unavailable when, as here, a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf. Id.; Dunlap v. United States, 250 F.3d 1001, 1008 (6th Cir. 2001)(“statute of limitations may only be tolled where the circumstances are both beyond the petitioner’s control and unavoidable even with due diligence”).
 
We also stated in our decision that the DHS opposed reopening. The respondent argues in his motion to reconsider that the DHS opposition was filed untimely, and the motion should have been deemed unopposed. Even were we to determine that the DHS opposition should not have been considered, we would have reached the same result. An exception to an untimely motion is made when a motion is “agreed upon by all parties and jointly filed.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(iii). This exception was clearly inapplicable in this case.
 
We also stated in our October 20, 2003, decision that the respondent had failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I & N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Specifically, we noted that the motion did not demonstrate that the respondent had filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority, or adequately explained why no filing was made. The respondent argues that he had, in fact, filed a disciplinary complaint, but was prohibited from divulging this to the Board. As the motion to reopen was filed late, we would have dismissed the motion to reopen as untimely even had the respondent complied with the Lozada requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We note, in any event, that the Board of Professional Responsibility of the Supreme Court of Tennessee determined that the respondent’s complaint was “unfounded”, and the complaint was “dismissed” (Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider, at Exh. 6).
 
*2 The motion to reconsider is, therefore, denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

The DHS’ request to file a late opposition is granted.

2004 WL 1167362 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMON PORTILLO IZAZAGA1 ROSALINDA PORTILLO VALDOVINOS RAMON PORTILLO VALDOVINOS MARIA DE ROSARIO PORTILLO VALDOVINOS
Files: A75 261 564 - San Francisco
A75 261 566
A75 261 567
A75 261 569
January 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Emmanuel Enyinwa, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James S. Stolley, Jr.
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on April 29, 2002, at which time the Immigration Judge’s March 23, 2000, decision was summarily affirmed without opinion. On August 13, 2002, the respondents filed a motion to reopen. The respondents acknowledge that the motion was not filed in a timely fashion, but they urge that the time period for filing the motion should be equitably tolled because they received ineffective assistance of counsel who represented them before the Board.2 The Immigration and Naturalization Service (the “Service,” now the Department of Homeland Security) has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied as untimely.
 
A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date that decision was rendered, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2)(2002). In the instant case, the Board issued a decision in each of the respondents’ cases on April 29, 2002. A motion to reopen would therefore have been due on or before Monday July 29, 2002. As the respondents acknowledge, their motion was untimely where it was filed on August 13, 2002, approximately 2 weeks after it was due. The respondents, however, claim that the time period for the filing of their motion should be equitably tolled based on the reasoning of the decision of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit in Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). Specifically, they allege that they were unable to file a motion to reopen in a timely fashion due to the ineffective assistance of attorney David Neumeister, who represented them before the Board.
 
The respondents’ allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are unpersuasive. First, the respondents have failed to indicate how attorney Neumeister’s assistance was ineffective with regard to the filing of their motion. The lead respondent admits that attorney Neumeister provided him with notice of the decision issued by the Board, which the lead respondent received approximately 20 days after the decision was issued. See Motion to Reopen, attached Declaration of Ramon Portillo dated June 22, 2002 (“Declaration”). He also admits that attorney Neumeister gave him an appointment in July 2002, and, although he does not specify the date of that appointment in his declaration, the motion itself states it was “almost three months from the” date of the Board’s decision, thus indicating that the scheduled appointment was within the 90-day period for filing a motion to reopen. Motion to Reopen at 3, and attached Declaration. The respondents, however, chose not to keep that appointment, and sought other representation. The lead respondent admittedly met with new counsel approximately 5 weeks before a motion was due, yet failed to file a motion to reopen in a timely fashion. These facts alone in no way establish ineffective assistance of counsel by attorney Neumeister with regard to the filing of a motion to reopen before the Board.
 
*2 In Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000), a case cited by the respondent to justify failing to comply with the requirements that the Board set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), the Ninth Circuit stated that “the Lozada requirements are generally reasonable, and under ordinary circumstances the BIA does not abuse its discretion when it denies a motion to remand or reopen based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel where the petitioner fails to meet the requirements of Lozada.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra, at 525. As discussed above, this case does not revolve “a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel” so as to excuse the respondents from satisfying the formal requirements of Lozada. Id at 525-26. The respondents have failed to set forth with particularity what their agreement was with attorney Neumeister with regard to any representation he would provide after issuance of the Board’s decision, to present any evidence that they informed attorney Neumeister of the allegations leveled against him or provided him with an opportunity to respond, or to indicate whether a complaint was filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. In light of the failure to comply with Lozada and the failure to present a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance, the respondents have not established that the time period for filing their motion to reopen should be equitably tolled under applicable Ninth Circuit precedent. Accordingly, the motion was filed out of time and will be denied as untimely.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

Nevertheless, in view of our decision in this matter, we need not determine whether his spouse should also have been included in the motion.

2004 WL 1167369 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN RAMOS
File: A73 479 251 - Newark
January 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Harlan G. York, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles Parker, Jr.
Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. An Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent on May 23, 2001. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38. The appeal was filed over a year late, on October 4, 2002.
 
The respondent urges the Board to accept the appeal out of time. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), in its brief, supports the decision of the Immigration Judge and takes no position on the untimely filing. The respondent claims that he trusted his former attorney, who represented him at the removal hearing, to file the appeal for him.1 However, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Xu Yong Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.2d 127, 132 (3d Cir. 2001)(Board’s requirements for making ineffective assistance of counsel claim are “a reasonable exercise of the Board’s discretion”). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, these cases require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. There is no convincing evidence that either former counsel was notified of the charges, or that a disciplinary complaint was made against former counsel. Rather, attached to the Notice of Appeal was respondent’s letter to the Essex County, New Jersey bar association, saying that he wished to bring a complaint against former counsel, and a letter from a Supreme Court of New Jersey arbitration committee, to the respondent, sending him information about their arbitration program. There is no evidence that a disciplinary complaint with the proper authorities was filed against former counsel. Moreover, while former counsel was provided with a copy of the arbitration committee materials, there is no convincing evidence that former counsel was notified of the charges against him.
 
We also note that the respondent concedes in the Notice of Appeal that he learned no appeal was filed when he was ordered to report for removal. Yet the respondent states in his affidavit that he was ordered to report for removal in May, 2002. Nevertheless, the appeal was not filed for 5 months. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 127, 134-135 (2d Cir. 2000) (party seeking to extend filing deadline based on theory of equitable tolling must show that he has acted with due diligence).
 
*2 We therefore find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.52, 1240.53, and 1241.31 (1999).

2004 WL 1167344 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANTANA-PEREZ, MARLEN
File: AXX XX4 615 - SAN ANTONIO
January 26, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dobbs, Dawn, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tilson, Allen Reid,
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge, dated November 15, 2002, denying the respondent’s untimely motion to rescind an order entered in absentia on March 5, 2001, and to reopen the removal proceedings. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). See also sections 240(b)(5)(C) and 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I& N Dec. 181 (BIA 2001); Matter of Guzman, 22 I& N Dec. 722 (BIA 1999).
 
The respondent’s argument based on ineffective assistance of counsel is not supported as required under Matter of Lozada, 19 I& N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F. 2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Lara v. Trominski, 216 F. 3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000) (in agreement with requirements). Further, there is nothing in the record to indicate that the respondent entered into an attorney/client relationship with either Catholic Charities of Tennessee, Inc. or Attorney Evaristo Martinez before the in absentia order was entered. See Respondent’s Application for Adjustment of Status (Form I-693), dated July 4, 2001; see also electronic mail, dated December 4, 2002 (stating respondent met Mr. Martinez at a social gathering in March 2001 in Kentucky). Thus, the respondent’s argument fails.
 
The respondent claims she did not receive oral notice of the consequences of failing to appear at her removal hearing in her native language, Spanish. The record reflects, however, the respondent’s signature on the Notice to Appear (Form I-862) indicating that she was personally served the Notice to Appear on April 1, 1999, that she was provided a list of organizations and attorneys who provide free legal services to aliens, and that she was provided oral notice in the Spanish language of the time and place of her hearing and of the consequences of failure to appear as provided in section 240(b)(7) of the Act (Exh. 1). We are not persuaded by the respondent’s belated claim to the contrary not only because the respondent has not offered an affidavit or any other statement under penalty of perjury indicating that she did not sign the Notice to Appear voluntarily and of her own free will, but also because the respondent’s diligence in notifying the Immigration Court of her address changes, which is also discussed in the notice of the consequences of failing to appear at the hearing in the Notice to Appear, and her several pro se motions to change venue, belie the respondent’s claim that she did not understand the consequences of failing to appear at her removal hearing. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 1167339 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FLORES CORTES, MARIA GUADALUPE
File: AXX-XX1-532 - LOS ANGELES
January 26, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hernandez, Michael J.
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 25, 2002. We concur with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not met the burden of proving statutory eligibility for the relief sought for the reasons set forth in the decision below. With respect to allegations of ineffective assistance by her former counsel in the Notice of Appeal, we find that the respondent has not established an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), which requires a detailed affidavit setting forth the agreement, an opportunity for the representative to respond to the allegation, and the filing of a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or an adequate explanation for the failure to file. See Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.2d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir. 2000), amended by 213 F.3d 1221 (9th Cir. 2000) (Lozada requirements generally reasonable unless facts relating to ineffective assistance claim apparent in the record). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the alien is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the alien fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the alien shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 1167291 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DONG MYUNG HAN EUN MI HAN LEEMO HAN LEETO HAN
Files: A76 141 596 - Newark
A76 141 597
A77 734 707
A77 734 709
January 22, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Don W. Pak, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa M. Golub
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (A76 141 596, A77 141 597)
Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - failed to comply with conditions of status (A76 141 596, A76 141 597)
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - No valid immigrant visa (A77 734 707, A77 734 709)
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondents appeal the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated January 17, 2003, denying their motion to reopen removal proceedings.1 The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge determined that the respondents failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel and, thus, denied their motion to reopen. On appeal, the respondents assert that they complied with the elements necessary to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as provided for in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
A motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and, (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003); cf. Matter of Lozada, supra, see also Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 132 (3rd Cir. 2000). Furthermore, the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance. See Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
In this case, the respondents failed to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. While the lead respondent submitted an affidavit, the affidavit fails to set forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the respondent. The affidavit does not establish whether counsel represented the lead respondent, his employer, or both. The affidavit does not provide any details regarding: a fee arrangement; whether the lead respondent, counsel or employer actually filed the labor certification application; whether the respondent and employer cooperated with counsel in order to timely respond to the request by the New Jersey Department of Labor; or, whether any agreement between the lead respondent and counsel required counsel to file a labor certification application by the filing deadlines contained within section 245(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). As such, the affidavit does not comply with the first prong of the Lozada requirements.
 
*2 While the lead respondent asserts that he complied with all the Lozada requirements and notified prior counsel of the allegations leveled against him and gave him an opportunity to respond, the record does not support his assertion. The motion to reopen is unaccompanied by any letter addressed to counsel informing him of the respondents’ allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel. Likewise, the state bar complaint does not indicate that a copy was sent to counsel by the lead respondent. Furthermore, the lead respondent’s complaint to the state bar was drafted on November 18, 2002. The state bar complaint was received by the Board on November 20, 2002, only 2 days after it was drafted. Thus, prior counsel was not afforded any opportunity to respond to the allegations before the Board was provided a copy of the state bar complaint. Consequently, the lead respondent failed to comply with the second prong of the Lozada requirements.
 
The lead respondent has also failed to demonstrate that he was prejudiced by attorney John Hykel’s alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. In this case, the record very clearly reflects that the lead respondent retained the services of two additional counsel, John J. Seehousen and Michael Choi, following his initial representation by John Hykel. There is no evidence in the record establishing that the lead respondent informed John J. Seehousen or Michael Choi that he had filed an application for labor certification and that he needed assistance with that application. The respondent has not made any claim that John J. Seehousen’s representation was ineffective. While the lead respondent states in his affidavit that he thought Michael Choi should have assisted him with his second labor certification application, he has not complied with any of the Lozada requirements to establish that Michael Choi provided ineffective assistance of counsel. As such, the respondents have failed to comply with the requirements of Lozada regarding any of the respondents’ different counsel. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s decision is not erroneous.
 
The respondents also assert in their motion to reopen that they are seeking adjustment of status. A motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application and all supporting documentation. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). However, the respondents have not submitted an application for adjustment of status or even established that they have filed a visa petition. Thus, the motion to reopen was properly denied by the Immigration Judge.
 
ORDER: The respondents’ appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondents have also submitted new evidence along with their appeal brief, however, they have not filed a motion to remand and we cannot consider any new evidence presented for the first time on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003(d)(3)(iv). This Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not create a record. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (B1A 1984). Consequently, we will adjudicate the appeal based upon the evidence contained in the record when it was before the Immigration Judge.

2004 WL 1167285 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMON CARMENATE
File: A75 308 870 - Chicago
January 22, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael H. Said, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on June 10, 2003, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen finding that his ineffective assistance of counsel claims had not been established on this record. The respondent has submitted a motion to reconsider the Board’s June 10, 2003, decision.1 The motion is denied.
 
When deciding a motion to reconsider, the Board considers the case as though the decision in the case on the record before us had never been entered. If the respondent was eligible for relief at the time of the original decision, then in reconsidering the decision, we treat his status as that which it had been at the time of the initial decision. “The very nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard.” Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991). We have reviewed the record, as well as both the Immigration Judge’s and this Board’s prior decisions. We have considered the respondent’s allegations concerning ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). However, we are not persuaded by the respondent’s arguments that reconsideration is warranted on that basis, as a matter of discretion.
 
In his motion to reconsider, the respondent asserts that former counsel was to have filed a brief in support of his appeal but failed to do so. He contends that a properly filed brief would have raised key issues on appeal. Ineffective assistance of counsel in an immigration proceeding is a denial of due process only “¿iºf the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from presenting his case.” Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491, 499-500 (9th Cir. 1986) (quoting Lopez v. INS, 775 F.2d 1015, 1017 (9th Cir. 1985). See also LeBlanc v. INS, 715 F.2d 685, 694 (1st Cir. 1983)(no need to remand based on ineffective assistance of counsel where “¿tºhere has been no violation of fundamental fairness”). Examining the matter before us, we find that there has been no showing that the respondent was prejudiced by his attorney’s performance. See Mohsseni Behbahani v. INS, 796 F.2d 249 (9th Cir. 1986) (to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, one must show prejudice). The respondent has failed to identify what was omitted that would have been included in his brief that was not included in his Notice of Appeal (form EOIR-26) that would have addressed how the Immigration Judge erred in his decision. Failure to file a brief is not per se prejudice. See Matter of Assad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2002) (respondent did not establish that this former counsel’s failure to file a timely appeal constituted sufficient prejudice to warrant consideration of his late appeal on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 1167268 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CATALINA MENDOZA DE LA CRUZ
File: A70 781 054 - Los Angeles
January 20, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elsa Martinez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lee P. Crystal
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on April 16, 2002, when we summarily affirmed an Immigration Judge’s decision which had denied the respondent’s request for cancellation of removal. We granted the respondent a period of 30 days to voluntarily depart. On May 16, 2003, the respondent filed with this Board a motion to reopen and remand, claiming that ineffective assistance of counsel had prevented her from previously requesting a remand for an adjustment of status. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). She further maintains that because the individual who was assisting her allegedly did not provide her with notice of this Board’s 2002 decision, the voluntary departure period should be equitably tolled. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has opposed the motion. The motion will be denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2.
 
The respondent maintains that she had married a United States citizen in 1999, shortly before the Immigration Judge denied her cancellation of removal claim.1 In a declaration accompanying her motion, the respondent stated that she retained the services of a “notary,” Judith Gill, for the preparation of the appeal. The respondent acknowledged that while she availed herself of Ms. Gill’s assistance during the prior proceedings, she retained an attorney to appear in Immigration Court. It is clear, from the respondent’s declaration that she understood that Ms. Gill was not an attorney, and that to be represented in Immigration Court the respondent knew she would have to retain someone with additional credentials.
 
Aside from the respondent’s failure to support her allegation that Ms. Gill defrauded her or made misrepresentations to her, Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (2002), we find that the respondent has not put forth an adequate claim under Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000). The respondent’s failure to notarize her own declaration or show that either the declaration or the complaint letter to Ms. Gill were actually sent or received, Reyes v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1126 (2003), is sufficient reason for us to conclude that the respondent has not put Ms. Gill on sufficient notice that her efforts have been challenged. We also note that while the complaint letter to Ms. Gill states that the respondent is pursuing legal action against Ms. Gill, the respondent has not submitted any evidence of this. Nor has the respondent shown that she has filed a grievance with any authorities, such as filing a complaint of unauthorized practice of law with the California State Bar. Id.
 
*2 More notably, however, the respondent stated in her declaration that, “Ms. Gill failed to advise me that I could have requested that the Board remand my case for the purpose of adjustment my status as early as December 2000.” This statement is contradicted by an earlier letter from the respondent. On November 6, 2000, the respondent sent correspondence to this Board, over her own signature, stating that she was married to a United States citizen and had been informed that she had to have her case remanded to the Immigration Court to adjust her status. She further indicated in the letter that an immigrant petition had been filed on her behalf, but she did not indicate the petition had been approved.2 In light of this letter, we find that we cannot give credence to the respondent’s Lozada declaration which alleges that Ms. Gill never informed her of such a procedure. Aside from the technical Lozada requirements, and aside from MS. Gill’s status as a non-attorney, without a credible declaration before us about what this representative did or did not do, we cannot conclude that the respondent was ineffectively represented.
 
Prior to our decision in Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002) (decided March 6, 2002), the respondent would not have been considered prima facie eligible to adjust her status as there was no evidence that the respondent’s visa petition had actually been approved. Accordingly, there was apparently nothing more for Ms. Gill to do for years after the appeal was filed, even assuming that the respondent had informed her that she had married a United States citizen. We are not inclined to find that Ms. Gill, a non-attorney, should have been aware of our decision in Matter of Velarde, supra, such that she should have known to file a motion to remand in the period of some 6 weeks between the March 6, 2002, publication of that case and our April 16, 2002, decision in this case. Moreover, there is still no evidence that Ms. Gill could have filed a successful motion which would have demonstrated the respondent’s prima facie eligibility for relief. The respondent, even now with new representation, has yet to show by clear and convincing evidence that there is a strong likelihood that her marriage is bona fide. Matter of Velarde, supra. We have no marriage certificate before us and we have no evidence, other than unsupported assertions, that the respondent’s husband is a United States citizen. This falls short of the requirements to overcome the statutory presumption that the marriage is fraudulent, and thus the respondent has failed to show prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status. Id. Consequently, there is no basis for reopening proceedings.
 
*3 As we cannot credit the respondent’s statements about what transpired between her and Ms. Gill, we are not inclined to find that the time limit on filing motions was tolled, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c), or that the respondent should be equitably excused for failing to voluntarily depart. See section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d). In this regard, we observe that notwithstanding the Immigration Judge’s directions, the respondent apparently failed for years, including at the time our last decision was rendered, to properly notify the government of her current address (Tr. at 24-25).3 See Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996) (deportation proceedings context). Cf. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (equitable tolling may be available where alien is defrauded and where alien exercises due diligence). Thus, the respondent’s motion is untimely, and she is also ineligible to adjust her status because she failed to voluntarily depart within the time period previously provided.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 1167282 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAGADEH PRATAPATI
File: A78 434 775 - Chicago
January 15, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Richard H. Trais, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Karen E. Lundgren
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is appealing the Immigration Judge’s January 29, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings so that his in absentia order of removal may be rescinded. The Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS”) agrees with the Immigration Judge’s decision. See DHS’s Appeal Brief.
 
We affirm and adopt the Immigration Judge’s decision and with the following comments the appeal will be dismissed. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the ¿I ºmmigration ¿Jºudge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The respondent argues that he did not receive notice of his January 9, 2002, hearing because his prior counsel, Ms. Sandra Greene, did not notify the Immigration Court of his new address in Chicago, Illinois. The respondent thus argues that he did not receive notice of the hearing because of his prior counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness, yet he has not complied with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for assessing whether an alien has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Before we determine if an attorney’s representation was ineffective and affected the outcome of his case we require that an alien first provide (1) an affidavit detailing the agreement between the movant and counsel; (2) a showing that the allegations of ineffective assistance were communicated to counsel and that he or she was given an opportunity to respond to them; and (3) a statement as to whether a complaint was filed against counsel with the appropriate disciplinary authorities and if not why not. We note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit, the circuit in which this case arises, has approved of such requirements. See Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498 (7th Cir. 2001).
 
The respondent has complied with none of these requirements despite being aware of them. This case is exactly the sort of situation which demonstrates the need for an alien’s compliance with Lozada. While it appears that Ms. Greene mailed a copy of a Notice of Hearing to the respondent at an address different from that which the respondent provided to the DHS upon his release from administrative detention, there is absolutely no showing the Ms. Greene ever undertook any responsibility to inform the Immigration Court of his new address. The respondent simply argues that Ms. Greene should have so informed the court. However, we note that by that time, venue in the respondent’s case had changed to Chicago, Illinois, from York, Pennsylvania. It is possible that Ms. Greene informed the respondent that he needed to make the Immigration Court aware of his new address, especially if he had not already found new counsel in Chicago.1 However, we do not know what Ms. Greene’s view of her obligation to the respondent was, or what agreements she believed existed between them, because there is no indication that the respondent has ever formally informed her that he has challenged her effectiveness in his motion to reopen or in his appeal to this Board.2 Thus, there has been no reason for her to respond to the respondent’s allegations. We have been presented with the respondent’s side of the story only.
 
*2 As we find that the respondent has failed to establish that he did not receive notice of his hearing due to the ineffectiveness of his prior attorney, we find that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Certainly nothing has been presented in writing that would inform us that Ms. Greene is aware of the allegations made against her.

2004 WL 1059658 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LARA-DIAZ, NOE
File: AXX-XX4-308 - SAN DIEGO
January 15, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kraus, Karla L., Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). In a decision dated December 11, 2002, an Immigration Judge found that the respondent was removable from the United States pursuant to section 212(a)(2)(C) of the Act because there was reason to believe that the alien was a narcotic trafficker. The Immigration Judge made this finding primarily based upon the respondent’s statements provided to Inspector Guzman of the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”), formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service, on September 14, 2001 (See Exh. 9). In the Notice of Appeal, the respondent argued that the written statement should not have been considered by the Immigration Judge because the officer did not testify at the hearing.
 
On this point, we note that in administrative proceedings the Federal Rules of Evidence are not controlling. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986); see also Martin-Mendoza v. INS, 499 F.2d 918, 922 (9th Cir. 1974), cert. denied, 419 U.S. 1113 (1975); Matter of Grijalva, 19 I&N Dec. 713 (BIA 1988). An Immigration Judge may receive into evidence any oral or written statement which is material and relevant to any issue in the case made by the alien or any other person during any investigation or examination. Id. Such evidence may be admitted as long as it is probative and its use is fundamentally fair so as not to violate due process standards. Fisher v. INS, 79 F.3d 955 (9th Cir. 1995). We have reviewed exhibit 9, and find nothing fundamentally unfair about its admission into evidence. The respondent indicated therein that the information provided was being given freely and voluntarily, and was true. In addition, we agree with the Immigration Judge there is no showing of egregious circumstances which transgress the notions of fundamental fairness and undermine the probative value of the evidence in question. Id. at 1051 n.5. We therefore find no error on behalf of the Immigration Judge to rely upon exhibit 9 in determining that the respondent was removable from the United States.
 
In addition, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent did not merit a favorable exercise of discretion in his application for cancellation of removal. That aspect of the Immigration Judge’s decision is also affirmed. The respondent further argued on appeal that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has not, however complied with any of the procedural requirements for establishing this contention. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Reyes v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1126 (9th Cir. 2003). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 1059674 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KRISTINA INGER GUSTA VS MCGRANE
File: AXX XX4 967 - Los Angeles
January 13, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert F. Graziano, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
Lodged: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact (withdrawn)
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration and Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on March 18, 2003, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision finding that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) met its burden of establishing that the respondent abandoned her lawful permanent resident status and ordering the respondent’s removal. On April 17, 2003, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider alleging that the Immigration Judge erred in not properly considering evidence of her property in the United States and her claim that she signed the Form I-407 (Abandonment of Lawful Permanent Status) under duress. On June 16, 2003, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen seeking a remand due to ineffective assistance of counsel and her recent marriage to an United States citizen. The motion to reconsider is denied. The motion to reopen is granted.
 
First, we deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider because it fails to specify any errors of fact or law in the Board’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). The respondent reiterates many of the same arguments that she already raised on appeal, and the Board considered those arguments but found no reason to reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision. In determining whether an alien has abandoned her lawful permanent resident status, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, under which jurisdiction the instant case arises, pointed out that the “relevant intent is not the intent to return ultimately, but the intent to return to the United States within a relatively short period.” See Singh v. Reno, 113 F.3d 1512, 1514 (9th Cir. 1997). Furthermore, the Ninth Circuit listed a range of factors to be considered in determining the alien’s intent, of which “property ties” is only one of the many factors. Id. at 1514-15. With regard to the respondent’s claim that she signed the Form I-407 under duress, we agree with the Immigration Judge that although the respondent testified that she did not voluntarily give up her lawful permanent resident status, the circumstances surrounding her signing of the form did not indicate evidence of duress. See Tr. at 86-94. For the above reasons, we find it necessary to deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider due to failure to specify any clear errors of fact or law in the Board or Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
*2 The respondent has also alleged in a motion to reopen that she received ineffective assistance of counsel from her former attorney, James Spirakis, Esquire. She has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 627 (BIA 1988), in that she has submitted an affidavit describing the agreement that was entered into with Mr. Spirakis and her complaints, she informed him of the allegations leveled against him through a letter and gave him an opportunity to respond, and she filed a complaint with the State Bar of California. See Motion to Reopen at Exhs. A and B. In the motion, the respondent claims that Mr. Spirakis did not properly develop the record of her property holdings, failed to timely submit documents to the Immigration Judge, failed to call one of her former husbands as a witness, and failed to produce evidence that another one of her former husbands, a Swedish national, had obtained a visa through the visa lottery in 1996. However, the Board has held that an alien is bound by the strategic decision of her counsel unless she can show that the counsel behaved so egregiously that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B- et al., 22 I&N Dec. 309, 310 (BIA 1998) (finding no ineffective assistance of counsel where the attorney’s insistence on corrborating evidence discouraged the aliens from applying for asylum). In our review of the record, we do not find that Mr. Spirakis’s representation rose the level of egregiousness envisioned in Matter of B-B-et al., supra.
 
Finally, the respondent seeks reopening to apply for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act based on her February 18, 2002, marriage to an United States citizen who has filed a visa petition on her behalf, relying on the Board’s decision in Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). With her motion, the respondent has submitted evidence regarding her marriage and a completed application for the relief sought. See Motion to Reopen at Exh. I. The DHS has filed no response to the motion. The motion to reopen to seek adjustment of status is granted.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is granted, proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE CONRADO ALVAREZ-RODRIGUEZ SANDRA ELENA ALVAREZ-TREJO WENDY CONCEPCION ALVAREZ-TREJO
Files: AXX XX4 492 - El Paso
AXX XX3 615
AXX XX3 616
January 12, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was rendered on August 4, 2003. The appeal was accordingly due on or before September 3, 2003. The record reflects that the Notice of Appeal was submitted to the Board of Immigration Appeals on September 5, 2003, but was rejected due to filing defects. The appeal was properly filed with the Board on September 25, 2003. Accordingly, we find the appeal is untimely as the record demonstrates that the appeal was not perfected within the requisite 30-day period. Also, we note from the Notice of Appeal that the respondents appear to be raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as the reason for the untimeliness of their appeal. The respondents have provided copies of payment receipts and mailing receipts to support their claim. However, in view of the evidence before us, we find that the respondents have not fully complied with the requirements as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); therefore, the Board need not address this issue. Thus far, we do not find this factor to be an adequate reason to accept this appeal by certification. The Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
We note that because we have dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).

2004 WL 1059691 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GERARDO FERRAZ MARTINEZ
File: AXX XX4 596 - Las Vegas
January 9, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Herbert Sachs, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On September 12, 2003, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s applications for relief, and the respondent reserved his right to appeal the decision. The respondent filed a Notice of Appeal on November 12, 2003. As the respondent recognizes, the appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38.
 
The respondent urges the Board to accept the appeal out of time. The respondent blames an attorney for failing to file a timely appeal. However, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, Matter of Lozada, supra, requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
Here, there is no evidence that the respondent gave the attorney the opportunity to respond to the allegations of ineffectiveness. Matter of Lozada, supra. Although the attorney submitted an appearance form with the Board, and we therefore recognize him as appearing on the respondent’s behalf, all the papers submitted to the Board were apparently prepared by the respondent himself. There is no statement or affidavit from counsel, giving his version of events. We cannot adequately evaluate whether former counsel bore any responsibility for the late-filed appeal. Moreover, there is no evidence presented to show that a complaint was filed with the proper disciplinary authority.
 
We find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.52, 1240.53, and 1241.31 (1999).

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
  Vacated by IN RE: MIGUEL NAVARRO-OROZCO, BIA, January 31, 2006
2004 WL 1059714 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIGUEL NAVARRO-OROZCO
File: A74 320 379 - Phoenix
January 8, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John Ayala, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sandra B. Myles
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 On October 24, 2003, counsel for the respondent filed the instant motion, following our order dated September 24, 2003, in which we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen filed with the Board on April 3, 2003. The Department of Homeland Security has filed an opposition to the motion. The respondent has styled his motion as a “Motion to Reopen Pursuant to Matter of Lozada,” and argues that due to ineffective assistance by his prior counsel he was prejudiced, and that the numerical limitations on motions to reopen therefore should not apply in this case. Even assuming, arguendo, that the respondent has met all of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988),1 he has failed to show that he was prejudiced by the actions of prior counsel.2 The respondent has not asserted that the actions of prior counsel prevented him from departing the United States voluntarily during the voluntary departure period provided by the Board, which formed the basis of the Board’s denial of his previous motion to reopen. More importantly, the respondent has failed to establish that he would now be successful on the merits of his claim for relief from deportation, such that reopening would be warranted.
 
An alien who seeks to remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). The new evidence submitted by the respondent indicates that he is now married to a United States citizen, and is the father of a United States citizen child of tender years (age 1) who is apparently in good health.3 It is highly unlikely that consideration of this new evidence would result in a grant of suspension of deportation.4 The respondent has not shown that there is a reasonable likelihood of success on the merits if proceedings were reopened. See Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000) (alien failed to show substantial likelihood that the result in the case would be changed if proceedings were reopened); see generally Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996).
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

In reaching our decision, we have considered that the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence). See also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000). In the instant case, we are unable to find that a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel has been established.

2

Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 3367 (BIA 1998).

3

The respondent’s motion is accompanied by an affidavit by the respondent, a citizen of Mexico, stating that he married on July 15, 2001, while his wife was a lawful permanent resident, and that his wife became a naturalized United States citizen on May 28, 2003.

4

The respondent has not submitted an application for adjustment of status, and indeed, has stated that he is not seeking such relief in this motion to reopen (Respondent’s Response to Opposition, at 4).

2004 WL 1059715 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUCIANO HERNANDEZ-MORAN
File: A92 896 254 - Chicago
January 8, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Miryam Antunez de Mayolo, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied.1 The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on September 4, 2003. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before December 3, 2003. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until December 12, 2003. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time. This untimely filing is only the latest in a history of failure by the respondent to file items in a timely manner and failure to exercise due diligence in pursuing his claims to relief in the United States.
 
The respondent alleges that he received ineffective assistance from prior counsel. The respondent has failed to meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (requirements for establishing claim of ineffective assistance of counsel), in that he has failed to provide a detailed description of his agreement with Mr. Banwo, has failed to provide evidence that Mr. Banwo had an opportunity to reply to his allegations, and failed to provide proof that he filed a complaint with the state bar.2 The respondent has also failed to show that application of the principle of equitable tolling would be warranted in this case, as he has failed to show that he reasonably relied on counsel and that the respondent himself exercised due diligence in pursuing his case.
 
Even if we were to find ineffective assistance of counsel in this case, or to apply equitable tolling to consider the motion despite its untimeliness, we would deny the motion in the exercise of discretion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent has failed to exercise due diligence. He concedes that he was unable to communicate with prior counsel due to a language barrier, but it appears that he made no attempts whatsoever to bring his own interpreter or to obtain other assistance. It is also apparent that the respondent failed even to provide his most recent address to Mr. Banwo, the Immigration Court, or the Board. The respondent filed the instant motion when he received a “bag and baggage” letter from the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), ordering him to report for removal on December 16, 2003. The respondent has failed to explain what he did in the intervening 4-1/2 years from the date of his removal order on July 13, 1999.
 
*2 The respondent has failed to submit any applications for relief. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). An alien who seeks to remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). See also Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000) (alien failed to show substantial likelihood that the result in the case would be changed if proceedings were reopened); see generally Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996). To date, the respondent has failed to establish that he qualifies for registry, cancellation of removal, temporary protected status, NACARA relief, or any other form of relief from removal. Thus, he has failed to establish that reopening would be warranted in this case.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

In any case, we would deny a motion to remand in the exercise of discretion as well, for the reasons stated in this order.

IN RE: GERARDO RAFAEL VILLASANA-SERRANO

January 2, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Royal F. Berg, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas O’Malley
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has filed an opposition to the motion.
 
The respondent states that the Board’s order dated September 25, 2003, did not specify how the respondent failed to comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We will now clarify. The respondent failed to comply with any of the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra,1 in his motion to reopen filed with the Board on April 29, 2003. With his April 29, 2003, motion to reopen before the Board, the respondent did not submit any documents regarding his ineffective assistance of counsel claim; he only submitted documents relating to the respondent’s family and community ties. The respondent has not pointed to any other “errors” in our prior order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1).
 
The respondent has asked that we reconsider our prior order. We have done so, and find that our prior order was correct and proper. We affirm our order dated September 25, 2003.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.

1

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Review Granted by Larin-Ulloa v. Gonzales, 5th Cir., August 24, 2006
2004 WL 1059643 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN LARIN-ULLOA
File: AXX XX0 131 - Los Fresnos
January 2, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa S. Brodyaga, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sean D. Clancy
Assistant District Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on August 20, 2003, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion asserts that his case should be reopened and proceedings terminated. Specifically, the respondent argues that he has complied with the requirements to establish ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and, thus, his previous pleading admitting his conviction for aggravated battery is essentially not binding on the respondent. Secondly, the respondent asserts that he is not removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), because the Eighteenth Judicial District, District Court, of Sedgwick County, Kansas has amended his judgment nunc pro tunc to reflect a conviction under Kansas Statutes Annotated (K.S.A.) section 21-3414(a)(1)(C) rather than his original conviction under K.S.A. section 21-3414(a)(1)(A).
 
A motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and, (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003); cf. Matter of Lozada, supra, see also Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000). Furthermore, the respondent must show that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance. See Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
In this case, the respondent failed to comply with the the requirements set forth in Maner of Lozada, supra. While the respondent submitted an affidavit, the affidavit fails to set forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representation counsel did or did not make to the respondent. The affidavit merely asserts that the respondent had limited English language abilities and that he lacked knowledge of K.S.A. section 21-3414. As such, the affidavit does not comply with the requirements of the first prong of the Lozada requirements.
 
*2 The respondent also claims that he complied with the second prong of the Lozada requirements and notified prior counsel of the allegations leveled against him and gave him an opportunity to respond. However, the record does not support this assertion. The respondent’s letter to prior counsel was drafted on May 20, 2003. It was mailed to the Board on May 22, 2003, and received at the Board on May 27, 2003. As such, prior counsel was not afforded an opportunity to respond to the allegations before the Board was provided a copy of the letter. Furthermore, the respondent’s allegation that counsel admitted that the respondent had been convicted under K.S.A. section 21-3414(a)(1)(A) was erroneous is without merit. At the time of respondent’s immigration hearing, the respondent had been convicted under K.S.A. section 21-3414(a)(1)(A) as reflected in the certified judgment (Exh. 2). The respondent had not appealed the judgment and, thus, it was a conviction for immigration purposes. Moreover, the respondent cannot collaterally attack his conviction in immigration proceedings. See Zinnanti v. INS, 651 F.2d 420 (5 th Cir. 1981) (immigration authorities have no power to adjudicate the validity of state convictions underlying deportation proceedings). Thus, the respondent’s letter to prior counsel fails to satisfy the requirements of the second prong of the Lozada requirements.
 
Additionally, the respondent has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s admission of the conviction. The conviction was independently established when the Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) offered into the record a certified copy of the judgment (Exh. 2). The respondent did not appeal the conviction. In addition, a state conviction may not be collaterally attacked in immigration proceedings. See Zinnanti, supra. Thus, counsel for the respondent did not prejudice the respondent by admitting to the state conviction. Consequently, the respondent has failed to comply with the requirements of Lozada. See Matter of Assaad, supra. Accordingly, the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel fails as a basis for reopening proceedings.
 
The respondent also asserts that the motion should be granted because the respondent’s prior conviction has been amended nunc pro tunc and his conviction under K.S.A. section 21-3414(a)(1)(C) is not an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(F). The respondent’s argument is without merit.
 
*3 While the respondent has submitted a copy of the amended judgment nunc pro tunc, the amended conviction constitutes a crime of violence under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, and thus, an aggravated felony. An aggravated felony is defined in section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101 (a)(43)(F), as a crime of violence (as defined in section 16 of title 18, United States Code, but not including a purely political offense) for which the term of imprisonment is at least 1 year. Section 16 of Title 18 of the United States Code defines “crime of violence” to mean (a) an offense that has as an element the use, attempted use, or threatened use of physical force against the person or property of another, or (b) any other offense that is a felony and that, by its nature, involves a substantial risk that physical force against the person or property of another may be used in the course of committing the offense.
 
The judgment nunc pro tunc reflects that the respondent was convicted of aggravated battery under K.S.A. section 21-3414(a)(1)(C). Specifically, K.S.A. section 21-3414(a)(1)(C) provides that aggravated battery is “intentionally causing physical contact with another person when done in a rude, insulting or angry manner with a deadly weapon, or in any manner whereby great bodily harm, disfigurement or death can be inflicted.” Furthermore, aggravated battery as described in K.S.A. section 21-3414(a)(1)(C) is a felony. See K.S.A. § 21-3414(b). “While respondent argues that the respondent’s conviction falls under the latter part of K.S.A. section 21-3414(a)(1)(C), “or in any manner whereby great bodily harm, disfigurement or death can be inflicted,” the judgment nunc pro tunc clearly reflects that the respondent committed the aggravated battery with a firearm. See Matter of Sweetser, 22 I&N Dec. 709 (BIA 1999) (court may look to conviction records when statute is divisible). Thus, the respondent’s conviction falls under the part of the statute stating that “intentionally causing physical contact with another when done in a rude, insulting or angry manner with a deadly weapon.” Use of a deadly weapon while intentionally causing physical contact clearly involves a substantial risk that physical force against another person may be used. Consequently, the respondent’s conviction for aggravated battery is a crime of violence as defined under 18 U.S.C. §16(b). Furthermore, the respondent was sentenced to a term of imprisonment of at least 1 year as reflected in the judgment nunc pro tunc. Therefore, the respondent’s conviction constitutes an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act and he is removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act.
 
*4 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 1059641 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARBELLA MORALES GARCIA
File: AXX XX5 371 - Los Angeles
January 2, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On November 22, 2002, an Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent. The respondent filed a Notice of Appeal on November 10, 2003. The appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38.
 
The respondent urges the Board to accept the appeal out of time. She claims that she trusted an individual, whom she thought to be an attorney, to file the appeal for her, but he did not file the appeal. The respondent fails to show prejudice resulting from her representative’s failure to file a timely appeal. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Rojas-Garcia v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 814, 826 (9th Cir.2003)(where alien deprived of direct appeal to Board, prejudice is presumed, but that presumption may be rebutted); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899-900, 901-03 (9th Cir. 2003)(respondent claiming ineffective assistance of counsel must show prejudice); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
The respondent was found ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229b(b)(1). Her application for cancellation of removal reveals that the respondent is ineligible for such relief, as she does not have a qualifying relative. Section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act.
 
In her Notice of Appeal, the respondent appears to raise an equal protection challenge to the removal statute. She says that she wants to show how she is “similarly situated” to other immigrants, but has been treated different under current immigration laws. Perhaps she claims, as others have, that the statute denies equal protection of the law, in that she was unable to seek suspension of deportation under section 244(a) of the Act, in the context of her removal hearing, where similarly-situated aliens were able to seek such relief if placed into deportation proceedings prior to the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Division C of Pub.L.No. 104-208, 110 Stat. 3009-546 (“IIRIRA”) taking effect. However, it is well-settled that the Board lacks the authority to rule on the constitutionality of the laws it administers. Matter of Fuentes-Campos, 21 I&N Dec. 905, 912 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Accordingly, we find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
