KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Padernal-Nye v. Gonzales, 9th Cir., May 24, 2005
2003 WL 23508533 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA LITA PADERNAL-NYE A.K.A. MARIA LITA NYE
File: AXX XX5 064 - Las Vegas
December 30, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles W. Baccus
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated December 30, 2002. The respondent argues that we should reopen proceedings because of her prior counsel’s ineffective assistance. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639, we held that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the claim reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not.
 
The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration, and Naturalization Service) opposes the respondent’s motion because there is no evidence that the respondent’s former counsel has been informed of the charges against him and given an opportunity to respond. The respondent contends that his current counsel notified the prior counsel of the charges by telephone.
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the circuit in which this case arises, recently issued a decision regarding Lozada’s notice requirement. In Reyes v. Ashcroft, 348 F.3d 1126 (9th Cir. 2003), the alien sent a letter regarding the ineffectiveness of the alien’s prior counsel to the California State Bar. The letter included a line stating “cc” and the prior counsel’s name. Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra. The court observed that the “cc” notation, at best, indicated that the alien intended on sending the prior counsel notice of the state disciplinary proceedings, but that the respondent offered no evidence that he sent the prior counsel notice of the state disciplinary proceedings. Id. at 1131. Furthermore, the court noted that the alien failed to give the prior counsel notice of his motion to reopen. Id. at 1131-1132. The court emphasized that the purpose of Lozada’s notice requirement is to encourage the prior counsel to contest the allegations of ineffective assistance so that the adjudicator can more accurately assess the claim of ineffective assistance. Id at 1132. Inasmuch as the alien provided no evidence that the prior counsel received the notice of the motion to reopen and did not give the prior counsel an opportunity to respond to the allegations, the court held that the alien failed to comply with Lozada’s notice requirement. Id.
 
*2 In the instant case, neither the letter to the disciplinary authorities nor the motion to reopen indicate that a copy was sent to the respondent’s prior counsel. We also note that the respondent’s current counsel has not provided an affidavit regarding his telephone call to the respondent’s prior counsel. Therefore, we find that the mere assertion that notice was given without any other evidence is insufficient to establish that the respondent substantially complied with Lozada’s notice requirement. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2003 WL 23508535 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALFREDO FRANCES-DE LAS ROSA
File: AXX XX7 917 - Dallas
December 30, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Juan Luis Burgos-Gandia, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James R. Vincent,
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We have jurisdiction over this timely appeal from the Immigration Judge’s April 15, 2002, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his case following a hearing held on February 13, 2002, in which the respondent was ordered removed in absentia. In the motion to reopen, filed March 20, 2002, the respondent’s counsel indicates that her office had relocated, and she did not receive the notice until March 19, 2002. An affidavit from counsel’s building manager corroborates this. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion, finding that notice to the respondent’s counsel was sufficient.
 
An order of removal issued following proceedings conducted in absentia maybe rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances,1 because he did not receive proper notice of the time and place of the hearing, or because he was in federal or state custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. See Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. §3.23(b)(4)(ii)(1999). See also Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995); Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993).
 
The Immigration Judge correctly indicated that notice of hearing served on the attorney of record constitutes notice to the alien. See 8 C.F.R. § 292.5(a). However, here it is undisputed that neither the respondent nor the respondent’s counsel received actual notice of the hearing before the hearing date. Though the Immigration Court did send the notice of the hearing to the last known address of the attorney of record, it is undisputed that the respondent’s attorney of record did not receive it because she moved without first notifying the Immigration Court of her new address.
 
Thus, the record reflects that, due to the respondent’s counsel’s negligence in informing the Immigration Court of her new address, the respondent was deprived of his right to a removal hearing. This clearly establishes ineffective assistance of counsel, which justifies reopening. While the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) argues that the respondent must comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), before being granted reopening, we find Matter of Lozada inapplicable here because the purpose of Lozada is to give attorneys notice of any claim of ineffective assistance being made against them. Here, the respondent’s current attorney of record is admitting the facts that constitute ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 We also note that there is no evidence that either the respondent or counsel were intentionally abusing the immigration process here. The respondent’s counsel filed the motion to reopen only one day after she allegedly received the notice of the February 13, 2002, hearing, and only five weeks after the hearing itself. And, because he is statutorily eligible for adjustment of status, the respondent would have no reason to circumvent the immigration process.
 
The respondent married a United States citizen in 1999. A United States citizen was born of the marriage, so the bona fides of this marriage are not in question. The respondent’s spouse filed a visa petition (Form I-130) on the respondent’s behalf on April 9, 2001. That visa petition was approved on February 15, 2002. In the record of proceedings is a copy of the respondent’s marriage certificate, a Form I-797, reflecting approval of the Form I-130, and an application for adjustment of status (Form I-485).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is sustained and proceedings are reopened, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

Where an alien’s claim is based on alleged exceptional circumstances, the motion to reopen must be filed within 180 days of the date of the order of removal. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i) (1997).

2003 WL 23508538 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DOMINGUEZ AGUILAR, MARIO
File: AXX-XX9-480 - LOS ANGELES
December 30, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gomez, Raul, Esq.
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 15, 2002. On appeal, current counsel for the respondent asserts that the respondent was lead by misrepresentations of a notario and a lawyer working in concert with a notario to apply for cancellation of removal to which he was not entitled and that the respondent’s due process and constitutional rights have been violated. In support of this claim, the respondent has submitted additional evidence on appeal consisting of documents showing that James R. Valinoti, Esquire has been suspended from the practice of law and an assertion by counsel that attorneys who appeared on behalf of the respondent are “believed to be” associates in Mr. Valinoti’s firm. No personal affidavit has been submitted by the respondent in support of this contention of ineffective assistance by a notario or attorney. The respondent requests that proceedings be terminated sua sponte in the interests of justice.
 
We have not considered the additional evidence submitted by the respondent on appeal. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57 (BIA 1984). This evidence does not establish prima facie eligibility for the relief sought and does not warrant a remand for further consideration by the Immigration Judge. To the extent that the respondent contends that his removal would be unconstitutional or violate due process, we note that the Board is not empowered to rule on the constitutionality of the statutes and regulations that we administer. See Matter of C-, 20 I&N Dec. 529, 532 (BIA 1992); Matter of Valdovinos, 18 I&N Dec. 343 (BIA 1982). We find that the respondent had a full and fair hearing and that the respondent has not established an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), which requires a detailed affidavit setting forth the agreement, an opportunity for the representative to respond to the allegation, and the filing of a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or an adequate explanation for the failure to file. See Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir. 2000), amended by 213 F.3d 1221 (9th Cir. 2000) (Lozada requirements generally reasonable unless facts relating to ineffective assistance claim apparent in the record). The respondent has not alleged or shown that he is eligible for any relief from removal other than voluntary departure, which was granted below. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the immigration Judge and the statute, the alien is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the alien fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the alien shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2003 WL 23508567 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LOURDES SORIANO-VINO
File: AXX XX2 826 - Los Angeles
December 23, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Elsie V. Hui, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jean Lin-Stevens
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reissuance of decision
 
*1 On June 23, 2003, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent moves the Board to reissue that decision. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent retained present counsel to file a petition for review of the Board’s June 23, 2003, decision before the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals. The Ninth Circuit received the petition for review on July 24, 2003, which was one day late and dismissed the petition as untimely.
 
The respondent filed the instant motion on October 14, 2003. With certain exceptions not relevant here, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion as untimely. Moreover, the DHS contends that the respondent did not comply with the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set down in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Even assuming that the respondent’s counsel rendered ineffective assistance by failing to file a timely petition for review in the Ninth Circuit, this Board cannot remedy ineffective assistance of counsel in a court of appeals. Whatever remedy the respondent has should be pursued in another forum in the federal court system. The Board has on occasion reissued its decisions, but only due to Board error or administrative problems involving receipt of the Board’s decision. In the instant case the respondent does not allege any Board error or any issue involving receipt of the Board’s decision.
 
Accordingly, we shall not sua sponte grant the respondent’s motion to reissue pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). See Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373, 380 n.9 (A.G. 2002); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (sua sponte reopening is limited to exceptional circumstances).
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen sua sponte is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2003 WL 23508656 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KRZYSZTOFF MAREK TRACZ
File: AXX XX7 561 - Chicago
December 23, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Malcolm B. Gerber, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Karen E. Lundgren
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration and reopening
 
In a decision dated February 4, 2003, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider his previous decision dated January 13, 2003, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent has appealed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On April 19, 2002, an Immigration Judge entered an order of removal against the respondent. The respondent submitted a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of his prior counsel. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on January 13, 2003, because the motion was not timely filed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Moreover, even assuming there was some type of equitable tolling, the respondent failed to comply with the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set down in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498, 501-02 (7th Cir. 2001); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reconsider, which the Immigration Judge denied on February 4, 2003. The Immigration Judge construed the motion to reconsider to be one to reopen since it attached documents in an attempt to belatedly comply with Matter of Lozada. We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision. These attached documents could have been produced in the earlier motion. The motion to reconsider was actually one to reopen. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991) (discussing the difference between motions to reopen and reconsider). Therefore, the respondent’s second motion exceeded the numerical limitations for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Furthermore, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s analysis that even if the second motion was one to reconsider, the respondent failed to comply with reconsideration standards. The respondent failed to show that the Immigration Judge erred in denying the motion to reopen.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed

2003 WL 23508563 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSEPH MOUTCHE
File: AXX XX4 346 - Baltimore
December 23, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ronald D. Richey, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Linda A. Dominguez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This record was last before us on February 20, 2003, when we affirmed, without opinion, the Immigration Judge’s January 25, 2002, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings so that his in absentia order of removal may be rescinded. On August 26, 2003, the respondent filed a motion to reopen his proceedings with the Board. The Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS”) opposes the motion on timeliness grounds and also opposes it on its merits. See DHS’s “Opposition of the United States Department of Homeland Security to Respondent’s Motion to Reopen.”
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen should have been filed within 90 days of our previous decision upholding the denial of his motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (2003).1 The respondent’s motion was filed more than 6 months after we issued our prior order. Accordingly, we will deny such motion as it was untimely flied.
 
However, even if we were to consider the respondent’s motion on the merits, it would still be denied. The respondent now asserts that his prior counsel, Mr. Edward K. Fogam, represented him ineffectively before the Immigration Court. The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements governing ineffective assistance of counsel arguments found in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). However, we do not find the respondent to have persuasively shown that Mr. Fogam’s representation was ineffective, or even it was, that he was prejudiced by such representation.
 
As noted by the DHS in its opposition, Mr. Fogam apparently still represents the respondent in his appeal of our prior order to the United States Court of Appeals for the Fourth Circuit. See DHS’s Opposition at 3; see also Mr. Fogam’s Respondent to Maryland Bar Counsel, dated August 14, 2003. In addition, the respondent has not persuasively explained why he failed to appear for his December 19, 2001, Immigration Court hearing. The respondent’s attendance at such hearing was not excused simply because Mr. Fogam had filed a motion to continue the respondent’s proceedings. See Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260, 262 (BIA 1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986). Although the respondent claims that Mr. Fogam never advised him to attend his master calendar hearing, Mr. Fogam asserted in response to such accusation that he did tell the respondent he had to attend, even though he (Mr. Fogam) was going to be out of the country on that date on business. See Respondent’s Memorandum to Maryland Bar Counsel, dated September 26, 2003; Mr. Fogam’s Letters to Maryland Bar Counsel, dated October 21, 2003, and August 14, 2003. The respondent has submitted no other evidence, such as a written communication between Mr. Fogam and him, to establish that Mr. Fogam advised him not to attend his hearing. Moreover, the Notice to Appear, Form I-862, which was mailed to him by the DHS, clearly informs him of the consequences in the event he failed to appear for his hearing. The respondent did not contest that he received such notice. Accordingly, we do not find that the respondent has established that Mr. Fogam’s representation caused his failure to attend his Immigration Court hearing or that he was prejudiced by any statements Mr. Fogam may have made to him.
 
*2 Accordingly, for the reasons described above, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent has only 90 days, however, after our prior order in which to file his motion to reopen with the Board.

2003 WL 23508570 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHUNHUA HWANG
File: AXX XX0 774 - Los Angeles
December 23, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frederick P. S. Whang
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on November 6, 2003, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 14, 2003, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered in her case on September 10, 2002, on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has submitted a timely motion entitled “motion for reconsideration,” in which she asserts that she now has sufficient “evidence” to meet the criteria set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The motion will be denied.
 
When deciding a motion to reconsider, the Board considers the case as though the decision in the case on the record before us had never been entered. If the respondent was eligible for relief at the time of the original decision, then in reconsidering the decision, we treat her status as that which it had been at the time of the initial decision. “The very nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard.” Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991). We have reviewed the record, as well as both the Immigration Judge’s and this Board’s prior decision. In the current motion, the respondent largely attempts to reiterate arguments already made in connection with her previous motion and already evaluated and rejected by this Board. A motion to reconsider is not a substitute for an appeal. It is not to be used as an opportunity for a second effort at proving factual allegations. See Matter of Guevara, 20 I&N Dec. 238 (BIA 1991); see also Matter of Cerna, supra. We are not persuaded by the respondent’s contentions that our prior decision was either legally, or factually, erroneous. The respondent has failed to demonstrate that reconsideration is warranted on that basis.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is denied.

2003 WL 23508677 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VILMA ESPERANZA MORALES ESTRADA MARIO LOPEZ ROBELORIO
Files: AXX XX0 477 - Miami
AXX XX6 471
December 22, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Mayra L. Calo, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on February 26, 2002. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before May 28, 2002. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until October 9, 2003. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.1
 
In their motion, the respondents have raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, explaining that they were ill-advised by Catholic Charities Legal Services to appear at their immigration proceedings pro se. A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, the motion must establish prejudice arising from the alleged failings of prior counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). We note the respondents have failed to show that Catholic Charities’ actions had such an adverse effect that respondents’ hearing before the Immigration Judge would have resulted in an otherwise favorable outcome. It is also noted that respondents were represented during the pendency of their appeal by Catholic Charities Legal Services, and that none of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, have been met.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

Respondents state the Board rendered its decision without setting a briefing schedule, and did not send a copy of its order to them. A review of the record indicates that a briefing schedule was indeed set in this matter, and that the Board’s order was mailed to them by way of their then counsel of record, Claire R. White of Catholic Charities Legal Services, and that it was not returned as undeliverable by the postal service.

2003 WL 23508680 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: ANAHIT APIKYAN
File: AXX XX5 276 - Los Angeles
December 22, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent in the instant case has filed a document titled “Motion to Reconsider Decision of the Board of Immigration Appeals.” Due to its content, it will be construed as a motion to reopen. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on December 3, 2002. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before March 3, 2003. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until September 5, 2003 The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.
 
In his motion, the respondent has raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, stating her former attorney did not notify her that the Board had rendered its decision. A review of the record reveals that no EOIR-27 (Notice of Entry of Appearance As Attorney Or Representative Before the Board of Immigration Appeals Executive Office For Immigration Review) was filed on respondent’s behalf. Consequently, the Board’s order was mailed directly to respondent at her last known address, and it was not returned as undeliverable.
 
We note further that a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, the motion must establish prejudice arising from the alleged failings of prior counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent here has failed to meet these requirements.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

2003 WL 23508693 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SOBHY SHEHATA HANNA
File: AXX XX8 763 - Detroit
December 17, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Scott Bratton, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
  
APPLICATION: Reopening.
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on March 21, 2003, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen proceedings, alleging ineffective assistance by his former counsel. The respondent’s motion will be denied.
 
The respondent has substantially complied with the requirements enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, we do not find that he has established that his former counsel’s failure to pursue a waiver, under section 237(a)(1)(H) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, violated his due process rights. See Huicochea-Gomez v. INS, 237 F.3d 696 (6th Cir. 2001) (finding that the failure to be granted discretionary relief does not amount to deprivation of a liberty interest). Specifically, the respondent has not established that, but for his former counsel’s advice regarding waivers, and his failure to apply for a 237(a)(1)(H) waiver, he would have been “entitled to continue residing in the United States.” Id. at 699. Hence, we do not find that the respondent has adequately established that his case warrants reopening due to ineffective assistance of counsel. Id.
 
Furthermore, we find that the respondent’s motion to reopen for 237(a)(1)(H) relief warrants denial as a matter of discretion. In this regard, we note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Sixth Circuit, in which jurisdiction the instant case arises, upheld a decision by this Board which stated that neither the courts nor the Board have “been lenient toward aliens who have flouted our immigration laws by engaging in fraudulent marriages to United States citizens or lawful permanent residents.” See Dallo v. INS, 765 F.2d 581, FN7 (6th Cir. 1985). Congress has emphatically expressed its disapproval of such conduct by the enactment of section 204(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(c), which precludes from immediate relative or preference status any alien who has previously entered, or attempted or conspired to enter, into a sham marriage for the purpose of obtaining an immigration benefit. Id. Here, the respondent not only attempted to adjust his status based on a fraudulent marriage, but he subsequently committed a further fraud by applying for a visa and indicating that he had never previously attempted to procure a visa or other documentation through fraud. See I.J. at 3; see also Exh. 2, allegation #4 (found). Thus, as the respondent has repeatedly disregarded United States immigration laws, and as Congress has denied preference status to aliens in similar circumstances who are outside the United States, it is reasonable to require the respondent to demonstrate substantial equities to warrant reopening of his removal proceedings. See Dallo v. INS, supra, at FN7. Mere statutory qualification for a 237(a)(1)(H) waiver should not suffice. Id. We do not find that the respondent, in this case, has demonstrated such substantial equities.
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2003 WL 23508710 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAFAEL MALDONADO-CEJA
File: AXX XX4 588 - Los Angeles
December 5, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Russell W. Miller, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF SERVICE:
 
Wealthia M. Rodriguez,
Assistant District Counsel
*1 In an order dated July 31, 2002, an Immigration Judge issued an in absentia decision, directing the respondent’s removal to Mexico. The respondent claims that he did not receive notice of the hearing. Through counsel, the respondent filed an “appeal” of the decision. We note that this is the second time that the respondent has been the subject of an in absentia order. See our orders of January 31, 2001; and February 7, 2002.
 
We lack jurisdiction to consider this “appeal.” An in absentia order, in circumstances such as this, may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen showing that the respondent did not receive notice of the hearing, or showing that he was in Federal or State custody, and the failure to appear was through no fault of his. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(ii).
 
Consequently, should the respondent choose to challenge the Immigration Judge’s decision, he must file a motion to reopen, demonstrating that he did not receive notice of the July 31, 2002, hearing, as he now alleges. The respondent will have the burden of explaining in his motion why his counsel appeared at the hearing and he did not, and why the provisions of 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5 and our holding in Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (notice to counsel constitutes notice to the alien), should not apply to him. Any motion based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel should comply with our holding in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.

2003 WL 23269899 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FRANCISCO JAVIER HURTADO A.K.A. FRANCISCO JAVIER HURTADO-GOMEZ
File: A90 009 048 - Los Angeles
December 4, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raul E. Godinez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa Anderson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), l&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)] - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)] - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent now appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen removal proceedings. We will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s motion.
 
This case was last before the Board when, on July 13, 2001, we remanded the case to the Immigration Judge to afford the respondent an opportunity to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). The record reveals that on November 28, 2001, the respondent appeared before the Immigration Judge without counsel and without an application for the section 212(c) waiver. The Immigration Judge then continued the proceedings to permit the respondent to return with counsel, and to submit the proper application. When the respondent’s counsel failed to appear for the scheduled hearing on November 30, 2001, and the respondent again failed to submit the proper application, the Immigration Judge deemed the application to be abandoned, and ordered the respondent removed to Mexico.
 
The respondent, through current counsel, filed a motion to reopen the proceedings on August 13, 2003, and the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposed the motion. On January 21, 2003, the Immigration Judge denied the motion on the ground that it was untimely filed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1) (February 28, 2003). In reaching his decision, the Immigration Judge noted that the circumstances of this case fail to correspond to the regulatory exceptions to the temporal limitation imposed on motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R § 1003.23(b)(4). This appeal followed.
 
On appeal, the respondent reiterates his argument, first put forth in the motion to reopen, that he was the victim of the ineffective assistance of his former attorney during the reopened proceedings in November 2001. According to the respondent, he appeared at the Immigration Court with his former attorney on November 30, 2001, but the attorney left the respondent, with assurances that he would return for the hearing, outside the court room prior to the hearing. The respondent also claims that an unidentified individual, who represented himself to the respondent as an attorney working on the case, appeared with the respondent at the November 30, 2001, hearing, and thereafter instructed the respondent to contact his former attorney with regard to an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent further asserts that his former attorney instructed him to pay a $120 filing fee for the appeal, and that he paid the fee. The respondent also asserts on appeal that the Immigration Judge failed fully to consider the documentation submitted in support of the motion.
 
*2 We note that the Immigration Judge correctly concluded that the respondent’s motion was untimely filed, and that the circumstances of this case fail to correspond to the regulatory exceptions under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4). The regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b), however, provides, in pertinent part, that “[a]n Immigration Judge may upon his or her own motion at any time (emphasis added) … reopen or reconsider any case in which he or she has made a decision.” Given the particular circumstances of this case, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
The Ninth Circuit has held that ineffective assistance of counsel may constitute exceptional circumstances for the failure to file a timely motion to reopen. See Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003). In the present case, we find that the respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements et forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.1 See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). Although the respondent filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, we note that the respondent’s former attorney has not responded to the complaint. We find it curious, however, that the respondent’s former attorney, who previously represented the respondent at the removal hearings before the Immigration Judge, informed the respondent’s current attorney on August 6, 2002, that his office “does not have the file for (the respondent).”
 
The Ninth Circuit has held that the statute of limitations for filing a motion to reopen is tolled from the time that an alien has been victimized by a negligent attorney until he learns of the negligence. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). In the present case, the respondent claims that he learned that his former attorney failed to file an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s November 30, 2001, decision, when, on July 25, 2002, he received instructions to present himself for removal. The record reveals that the respondent subsequently engaged his present attorney, and he filed the motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge on August 13, 2002.
 
The respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and he has shown that he filed the motion to reopen within 90 days of the date on which he claims to have learned of his former attorney’s negligence. If the respondent’s failure to file a timely application for the section 212(c) waiver, and his failure to file a timely Notice of Appeal, were the result of his former attorney’s misconduct, the respondent would have suffered prejudice as a result of the attorney’s mishandling of this case. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003). In light of these factors, we will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Upon remand, the Immigration Judge will have an opportunity to fully consider the documentation submitted in support of the motion, and to render a new decision. If the Immigration Judge renders a decision favorable to the respondent, the respondent will again have the opportunity to apply for the waiver of inadmissability under section 212(c) of the Act pursuant to our July 13, 2001, order.
 
*3 The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

In Matter of Lozada, supra, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved applicant setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the applicant in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given, an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640.

2003 WL 23269873 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GEORGE LEGASPI MIOTEN
File: A38 562 921 - Buffalo
December 3, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Anne E. Doebler, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carla J. Hengerer
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)] - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Termination; relief under section 212(c)
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines, appeals the decision of the Immigration Judge dated March 5, 2002. In that decision, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent was inadmissible on the basis that he had been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude.1 The Immigration Judge further found that the respondent had abandoned his application for relief under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), when he failed to file that application by September 24, 2001, the date previously set as the filing deadline. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the crime at issue, “felonious restraint” in violation of title 9, section 22.30 of the Guam Code, is divisible, and that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) failed to establish that the respondent pled guilty to that portion of the statute that may be a crime involving moral turpitude. We have held that moral turpitude refers generally to conduct that is inherently base, vile, or depraved, and contrary to the accepted rules of morality and the duties owed between persons or to society in general. See Matter of L-V-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 594 (BIA 1999); Matter of Tran, 21 I&N Dec. 291 (BIA 1996). A conviction for unlawful restraint requires a showing that one has acted “knowingly” and that he either restrained another person exposing that person to risk of serious bodily injury, or that he held another person in conditions of involuntary servitude. See title 9, section 22.30 of the Guam Code. We conclude that a conviction under either aspect of this statute involves a motive that is both vicious and corrupt, and violates the duties owed between persons, and therefore, is a crime involving moral turpitude. Moreover, the record clearly establishes that the respondent pled guilty to the third charge listed in the indictment in his case. The third charge states that the respondent knowingly restrained another unlawfully in circumstances exposing her to risk of serious bodily injury in violation of 9 G.C.A. § 22.30(a). The respondent appears to acknowledge that a violation of subsection (a) of § 22.30 constitutes a crime involving moral turpitude. For these reasons, we find no merit to the respondent’s argument concerning the statute’s divisibility.
 
*2 The respondent also contends that he should be allowed to apply for a waiver of inadmissiblity under section 212(c) of the Act. The record establishes that on July 25, 2001, the Immigration Judge set September 24, 2001, as the filing deadline for the respondent’s section 212(c) application (Tr. at 11). The application was never filed. At the March 5, 2002, hearing, the respondent was represented by new counsel who advised the Immigration Judge that the respondent’s prior counsel was no longer employed at the legal agency that was representing the respondent, and further that prior counsel had failed to note in the respondent’s file that his section 212(c) application was due on September 24, 2001 (Tr. at 14-16). Counsel requesteda continuance to allow the application to be filed, and the request was denied (Tr. at 15-16). On appeal, the respondent contends that he should still be allowed to file a section 212(c) application because he previously received ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, supra, we held that such claims require: (1) an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the respondent state whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id.
 
In the instant case, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit or filed a complaint with the bar association against his former attorney. He has not offered any explanation as to why he did not file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Esposito v. INS, 987 F.2d 108 (2d Cir. 1993) (finding that alien gave a reasonable explanation for why he did not file a complaint with the disciplinary authority). He has also not provided any evidence that proves he would have been granted a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) had his former counsel submitted the application to the Immigration Court. Based upon this lack of proof, we are unable to find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements for proving that he was ineffectively represented or was prejudiced by his former attorney in these proceedings.
 
*3 On appeal, the respondent challenges the fact that he was charged as an alien seeking admission to the United States under section 101(a)(13) of the Act, even though he was a lawful permanent resident of the United States. To the extent that the respondent challenges our decision in Matter of Collado, 21 I&N Dec. 1061 (BIA 1998), we decline to revisit that decision. To the extent that the respondent challenges the constitutionality of section 101(a)(13) of the Act, this Board has no authority to rule upon the constitutionality of the laws we administer. See Matter of Valdovinos, 18 I&N Dec. 343 (BIA 1982).
 
Finally, we observe that in the Immigration Judge’s oral decision, as well as in his written order, the respondent was ordered removed to Guam. However, the respondent is a native and citizen of the Philippines, and the country designated for removal at the respondent’s hearing was the Philippines (Tr. at 10). The Immigration Judge’s order will, therefore, be corrected so as to order the respondent removed to the Philippines.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s country of removal is amended from “Guam” to “the Philippines.”
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident on May 31, 1984. He was charged as an alien seeking admission to the United States upon his return to this country following a trip to Canada. See section 201(a)(13) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(13).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KARL MARX FERNANDEZ WAITE
File: AXX XX8 435 - New York
December 3, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Bradford H. Bernstein, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Susan Egan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on July 7, 2003, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider. On August 13, 2003, the respondent filed a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, and requesting the termination of proceedings on the basis that he is a United States citizen. On September 25, 2003, the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) filed a response requesting a remand to the Immigration Court for an evidentiary hearing. The respondent’s motion will be granted. The DHS’ motion is granted.
 
In support of his motion, the respondent submitted, inter alia, photocopies of his birth certificate, his United States passport, his Immigrant Visa and Alien Registration Form (Form I-73) and his mother’s naturalization certificate. Specifically, the respondent contends that he “automatically acquired citizenship pursuant to former § 321 upon his mother’s [n]aturalization when he was 16-years-old” (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 3). Additionally, the respondent asserts that his former counsel’s failure to recognize the respondent’s aforementioned acquisition of United States citizenship materially prejudiced his case (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 10-13). We note that the respondent filed evidence that he complied with each of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Through its motion, the DHS disagrees with the respondent’s contention that he is a United States citizen. Specifically, the DHS asserts that the respondent was not able to solely derive citizenship through his mother. Additionally, the DHS argues that it is possible that the United States passport was issued to the respondent in error. Accordingly, the DHS requests that we remand this matter to the Immigration Court for an evidentiary hearing.
 
A motion to reopen must state “new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent must also establish his prima facie eligibility for the requested relief. A motion to remand is governed by the same regulatory requirements that govern motions to reopen. See Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 2000).
 
In light of the evidence of record, the facts show that it is worthwhile to develop the issues at a hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for a full evidentiary hearing. The following orders will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The DHS’ motion to remand is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with this order.

2003 WL 23269894 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CARMEN LUISA MINIER
File: AXX XX4 461 - San Juan
December 1, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose Guillermo Gonzalez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jorge A. Ramos
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)] - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Continuance
 
In a decision dated July 23, 2002, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged and ordered her removed from the United States. The respondent has appealed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent married a United States citizen who filed a visa petition on her behalf. The respondent also filed an application for permanent residence. The Immigration and Naturalization Service (now the Department of homeland Security “DHS”) issued a Notice of Intention to Deny the visa petition on July 2, 1999. The DHS observed numerous discrepancies between the respondent’s and her husband’s interviews. The respondent’s husband did not respond and on January 5, 2000, found the marriage was not bona fide and denied the visa petition. The respondent’s application for permanent residence was also denied.
 
On appeal the respondent blames the inactions of two former attorneys who she claims represented her during the visa petition proceedings. The respondent sought a further continuance to adjudicate a second visa petition, and the Immigration Judge denied the motion. The immigration Judge noted that the respondent had failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, and she had waited over one year and seven months since she was placed in removal proceedings to file her visa petition. (Tr. at 18). An Immigration Judge, in his discretion, may grant a motion for continuance for good cause shown. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. Based upon the facts, we find that the Immigration Judge did not abuse his discretion in denying the motion for another continuance due to the delay in filing the second visa petition and the fact that the DHS had already denied the first one.
 
The respondent also claims that prior attorneys rendered ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has apparently filed bar complaints against two attorneys. However she has failed to comply with any of the other requirements. As we stated in Matter of Lozada, at 639, “before allegations of ineffectiveness are presented to the Board, former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Any subsequent response from counsel, or report of counsel’s failure or refusal to respond should be submitted with the motion” (emphasis added). We noted that the high standard which we enunciated was necessary if the Board is to have a basis for assessing the substantial number of claims of ineffective assistance of counsel that come before us. Id. The respondent did not submit an affidavit and lay out the agreement between the parties, and the respondent did not give these attorneys the opportunity to respond to the allegations made against them. Finally, the respondent has not shown that she was prejudiced by their representation. The respondent has not established that the DHS erred in denying the visa petition and adjustment application.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

2003 WL 23269917 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MEI CHUNG OU
File: A76 280 000 - Los Angeles
November 28, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL/MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Wellington Y. Kwan, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Chimarusti
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On November 5, 2001, an Immigration Judge issued an order of removal against the respondent. The respondent filed a Notice of Appeal over a year and a half later, on August 12, 2003. As the respondent recognizes, the appeal was filed very late. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(b). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the respondent’s motion that the Board accept the untimely appeal.
 
The respondent’s motion blames a legal “service center”, and an attorney, for the late submission of the notice of appeal. He claims that representatives of the service center told him that an appeal had been filed, and he did not learn otherwise until July, 2003. This case arises within the jurisdiction of the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals. That court has determined that time and numerical limitations on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled, based on actions taken by non-attorneys purporting to provide legal representation in immigration cases. Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018, 1020-22 (9th Cir. 2002); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 2000). However, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988)(as reaffirmed by Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003)). See Fajardo v. INS, supra, at 1020-21; Varela v. INS, supra, at 1239; Lopez v. INS, supra; at 1100. Specifically, the respondent did not give the “service center” or the attorney the opportunity to respond to his allegations. Fajardo v. INS, supra, at 1021, fn.5, citing Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.
 
We acknowledge that in Dearinger ex rel. Volkova, 232 F.3d 1042, 1045-56 (9th Cir. 2000), the court found that prejudice is presumed in a situation where counsel filed an appeal with that court 1 day late. See also Roe v. Flores-Ortega, 528 U.S. 470, 482 (2000) (holding that “counsel’s constitutionally deficient performance depriv[ing] a defendant of an appeal that he otherwise would have taken” establishes a “successful ineffective assistance of counsel claim entitling him to an appeal” without any additional showing of prejudice). However, given the respondent’s failure to give his former representatives the chance to respond to the allegations, we are unable, to evaluate the culpability of the representative for the late filing in this case.
 
*2 Accordingly, we find that the appeal is untimely. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the records are returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.

2003 WL 23269920 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TUNDE SMITH
File: AXX XX3 876 - Houston
November 28, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Imdad A. Seehar, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mark Evans
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on July 28, 2003, at which time we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 31, 2003. The respondent has now timely moved to reopen the proceedings and remand to the Immigration Court for the presentation of new evidence on the basis of ineffective assistance of previous counsel. We observe the respondent’s motion was properly served on the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service), and the DHS has indicated its opposition to this motion. The motion will be granted and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this decision.
 
It appears the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (e.g., copy of grievance complaint filed with the State Bar of Texas, copy of agreement as to fees and work to be performed, etc.), and demonstrated the actions of the previous counsel below precluded him from fully presenting or developing his claim below resulting in prejudice requiring reopening of the proceedings. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2001). At the January 15, 2003, hearing the Immigration Judge had informed respondent’s counsel that he should be present on January 31, 2003, or submit reasons to the court for his absence along with evidence that he had cases scheduled elsewhere (Tr.at11). The respondent’s counsel filed a motion to continue on January 30, 2003, the day before the scheduled hearing, that was not accompanied by evidence of counsel’s scheduling conflicts. When the respondent appeared without counsel at his January 31, 2003, hearing, without a withholding application that was to have been prepared by his attorney, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent had abandoned this and all other forms of relief and ordered him removed from the United States. In our decision, the Board noted that even after the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent’s counsel failed to provide any reasons for the failure to file the application as directed. On this record, we find support for the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel that precluded him from fully presenting or developing his claim for relief, so as to warrant reopening of the proceedings.
 
Furthermore, we are not persuaded by the DHS’ other arguments in opposition to the respondent’s motion. Initially, we determined in Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002), that a properly filed motion to reopen for adjustment of status based on a marriage entered into after the commencement of proceedings may be granted in the exercise of discretion. Consequently, although the respondent’s pending immediate relative visa petition filed with the DHS remains unadjudicated, the presentation of clear and convincing evidence indicating a strong likelihood that the marriage is bona fide will not preclude granting of the requested relief. In addition, as the respondent had a previous immediate relative visa petition filed on his behalf terminated by divorce while his application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act was pending, the regulations provide that he is a “grandfathered alien” and his new application under section 245(i) of the Act will be considered timely based on circumstances that have arisen after the time of filing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.10.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s July 28, 2003, is vacated, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

2003 WL 23269868 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EZEQUIEL MIRAMONTES-ARTEAGA
File: AXX XX9 677 - Los Angeles
November 25, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
J. Thomas Logan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jean Lin-Stevens
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; section 212(c) waiver
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s March 17, 2003, decision denying his motion to reopen deportation proceedings. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7) (2003). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On January 13, 1998, the respondent was ordered deported by an Immigration Judge based on his 1994 conviction of a drug trafficking aggravated felony. It appears that the Immigration Judge also pretermitted the respondent’s request for a waiver of deportability under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), based upon the Attorney General’s decision in Matter of Soriano, 21 I&N Dec. 516, 533 (A.G. 1997). No appeal was filed with this Board from the Immigration Judge’s decision and it appears that the final deportation order was executed.
 
On January 24, 2003, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge in which he argued that the January 1998 order was issued in derogation of his right to a fair hearing because the Immigration Judge (1) erroneously concluded that his 1994 criminal conviction involved an aggravated felony; (2) failed to inform him of his right to seek a section 212(c) waiver; and (3) led him to believe that it would be futile to file an appeal. Accordingly, the respondent requested an opportunity to submit an application for a section 212(c) waiver. The Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely under both the general motions regulations and the particular regulations governing applications for section 212(c) relief filed by aliens with final orders of deportation. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1) and 1003.44(f). Although not entirely clear, it also appears that the motion was barred because it was filed after the respondent’s physical departure from the United States under a final order of deportation — an act which brought the deportation proceedings to a close. Cf. Matter of Okoh, 20 I&N Dec. 864, 865 (BIA 1994).
 
On appeal, the respondent does not challenge the Immigration Judge’s decision on its face, but instead argues for the first time that both his 1998 deportation proceedings and his 2003 proceedings with respect to the motion to reopen were tainted by ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, he asserts that the attorney who represented him before the Immigration Judge in 1998 negligently failed to file an appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision, and that this failure prejudiced the respondent in light of the Supreme Court’s subsequent decision in INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). Moreover, the respondent appears to argue that the attorney who filed his motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge in January 2003 was ineffective when he failed, in the motion, to raise the issue of prior counsel’s ineffectiveness.
 
*2 The respondent concedes that he has not complied with the threshold procedural requirements governing claims of ineffective assistance of counsel in immigration proceedings, as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Respondent’s Opening Brief at 8. However, the respondent appears to argue that his obligation to comply with the Lozada factors is excused because he has complied with them in the context of a criminal trial in a United States District Court in California. We disagree. Obviously, in order for this Board to assess the merits of the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim and to determine whether a plenary rehearing is necessary, the evidence supporting that claim must be submitted to the Board, not to a United States District Court. Furthermore, we see no indication of compliance with Lozada, even at the District Court level, in connection with the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance against the attorney who filed his 2003 motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge. An alien’s failure to comply with the Lozada requirements, where it would have been reasonable to expect such compliance, constitutes a failure of proof, since such an omission effectively prevents the Board from assessing the substantive merits of the claim. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, — F.3d —, 2003 WL 22660175 (9th Cir. Nov. 12, 2003).
 
Because the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim is defective on its face and because the respondent has identified no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s decision, will affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen and dismiss the respondent’s appeal.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD
 
2003 WL 23269868 (BIA)

2003 WL 23508509 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VISHUNLALL GOBIND
File: AXX XX2 236 - New York
November 24, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ilona Stanley, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: See. 241(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
 
Lodged: See. 241(a)(1)(G)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(G)(i)º - Marriage fraud
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This matter was last before the Board on July 22, 2003, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen these deportation proceedings, and his motion to reconsider our March 28, 2003, dismissal of the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his waiver application under section 216(c)(4)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. 1186(c)(4)(B). The respondent thereafter filed a timely motion to reconsider our July 22, 2003, denial of his motion to reopen. This Board has jurisdiction over the respondent’s current motion to reconsider pursuant to federal regulations at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). The motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
We find that we did not err in requiring a showing of “misfeasance” for the respondent to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel through his prior motion to reopen. See Decision of the Board of Immigration Appeals, dated July 22, 2003, at 2; Respondent’s Memorandum of Law in Support of Motion to Reconsider, dated August 20, 2003, at 5-7. Even the respondent has conceded that the standard for determining ineffective assistance of counsel “can be satisfied by nonfeasance as well as misfeasance. See Strickland v ¿º. Washington, 466 U.S. 668 (1984) (noting that claim for ineffective assistance of counsel must specify acts or omissions which were not the result of reasonable professional judgment)…” See Respondent’s Memorandum of Law in Support of Motion to Reconsider, dated August 20, 2003, at 6. Moreover, our use of the term “misfeasance” (the improper performance of some act which a person may lawfully do) in our prior denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen implicitly included both “nonfeasance” (the omission of an act which a person ought to do) and “malfeasance” (the doing of an act which a person ought not to do at all). See Black’s Law Dictionary, 6th Edition, at 1000; Respondent’s Memorandum of Law in Support of Motion to Reconsider, dated August 20, 2003, at 5-7. We further find that our requirement of “misfeasance” for ineffective assistance of counsel is in compliance with precedential case law. See Strickland v. Washington, supra; Zhao v. United States Department of Justice, 265 F.3d 83 (2nd Cir. 2001); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
*2 In addition, we find that we did not mischaracterize the respondent’s former counsel’s actions when we concluded, in our denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen, that they were “reasonable tactical decisions.” It is not for this Board to speculate concerning former counsel’s motivation for not submitting the evidence which the respondent sought to submit subsequent to his hearing. Rather, it is clear that the respondent’s former counsel was aware of the evidence and made a strategic choice not to submit it to the court. See Respondent’s Memorandum of Law in Support of Motion to Reconsider, dated August 20, 2003, at 7-9. We note that the letters from the respondent’s friends and family members, which were submitted with his motion to reopen, could have previously been obtained and submitted at his former hearing, and the letters from his former wife do not persuade us that “the qualifying marriage was entered into in good faith by the alien spouse …” See section 216(c)(4)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider and Reopen, dated April 26, 2003, and attachments.
 
Therefore, we find that none of the respondent’s contentions in his motion to reconsider reveals a factual or legal aspect of his case that was improperly overlooked, or presents a new argument which persuades us to alter our prior decision. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.

FOR THE BOARD
