2003 WL 23508503 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VICTOR RAFAEL FELIZ
File: AXX XX3 844 - Oakdale
November 24, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Glenda M. Raborn
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Lodged: Sec. 241(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c)
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on January 18, 2001, when we remanded to provide the respondent an opportunity to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C, § 1182(c), pursuant to the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit’s decision in St. Cyr v. INS, 229 F.3d 406 (2d Cir. 2000), aff’d, INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). On October 17, 2001, an Immigration Judge administratively closed the case and allowed the respondent’s former attorney, Leon Quintero, Esquire to withdraw as counsel of record. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) filed a motion to recalendar proceedings on June 17, 2001, and the case was reopened. The respondent now appeals the Immigration Judge’s August 28, 2003, decision denying his application for a waiver under section 212(c) of the Act. The appeal is dismissed.
 
The record shows that the respondent plead guilty to one count of conspiracy to distribute and possess with intent to distribute cocaine, and was convicted on October 13, 1995. See Exh. 4 (conviction records). In her decision, the Immigration Judge found the respondent to be statutorily ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act because he had served over 5 years of imprisonment as a result of his conviction. The respondent contends on appeal that he was eligible for the waiver in December of 1997 when his case was first before an Immigration Judge and he was found ineligible pursuant to Matter of Soriano, 21 I&N Dec. 516 (BIA 1996; A.G., Feb. 21, 1997), and again in October of 2001 when the second Immigration Judge administratively closed the case. He argues that the first immigration Judge’s decision in 1997 violated his due process rights under the Constitution. He further argues that in 2001, he received ineffective assistance of counsel from his former attorney and the DHS engaged in affirmative misconduct. See Resp. Appeal Br. at 14.
 
We find that the Immigration Judge did not err in finding the respondent statutorily ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act because he had served over 5 years of imprisonment as a result of his conviction. See Matter of Davis, 22 I&N Dec. 1411 (BIA 2000) (reaffirming the existence of the 5-year bar for aliens whose section 212(c) applications are considered under the pre-1996 law). Although the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from Mr. Quintero and he has submitted a letter from his wife in support of that claim, he has failed to comply with the requirements as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, the Board does not have the authority to apply the doctrine of equitable estoppel against the DHS so as to preclude it from undertaking a lawful course of action that it is empowered to pursue by statute and regulation, and the respondent has not proven that the DHS engaged in the type of “affirmative misconduct” necessary for equitable estoppel to apply. See Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335 (BIA 1991). Finally, we find that the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge’s denial of his application for a 212(c) waiver in 1997 violated his constitutional due process rights is without merit, as the Immigration Judge was properly applying the law as it stood at the time.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2003 WL 23508451 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMIR ZIANI
File: AXX XX0 547 - Miami
November 21, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Akhtar Hussain, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on September 24, 2003, when we dismissed the respondent’s untimely appeal for lack of jurisdiction and found that the respondent had failed to establish ineffective assistance of his prior counsel as the reason for his failure to file a timely appeal. In our decision, the Board determined that the respondent had failed to fully comply with the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel, as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec, 637 (BIA 1988). On October 15, 2003, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider in which he submits additional evidence in support of his argument that the reason for his failure to file a timely appeal was due to his prior attorney’s ineffective assistance. The respondent’s motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in the Board’s decision in Matter of Lozada, supra, aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Matter of Lozada, supra, we held that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must (1) be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) provide evidence that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) indicate whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. In addition to these requirements, an alien alleging ineffective assistance of counsel must also show that he or she was prejudiced by the actions or inactions of counsel. See Matter of Aassad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
In the present appeal, the respondent has complied with the three-step procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent has provided the required affidavit, informed former counsel of the allegations against her, and notified the appropriate disciplinary authorities in the State of Florida. However, we must deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider because he has not shown the required prejudice stemming from his prior counsel’s inaction. See Matter of Aassad, supra, at 561.
 
*2 In this matter, the respondent asserts that prior counsel’s failure to file a timely appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen removal proceedings is prejudicial. We disagree. We find that the respondent has not reasonably shown any prospect for establishing that heavy traffic constitutes an exceptional circumstance, which would have warranted rescinding the Immigration Judge’s in absentia removal order and reopening the respondent’s removal proceedings. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1) (“The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or serious illness or death of the spouse, child, or parent of the alien, but not including less compelling circumstances”). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.

2003 WL 23508468 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NEIL SPENCER WALTERS
File: AXX XX0 359 - Buffalo
November 20, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel M. Griebel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent received proper notice of his hearing scheduled for August 24, 1993, and he failed to establish exceptional circumstances based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988),
 
On appeal the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge improperly denied his motion to reopen filed on August 4, 2003, as he now seeks reopening for consideration of his eligibility for adjustment of status based on his marriage to a United States citizen. The respondent states that he is now eligible to pursue adjustment of status since his visa petition has been approved. An alien is not required to rescind his or her deportation order under section 242B of the Act if he or she is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). Rather, the motion to reopen is only subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3).1 Under these standards, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision of August 24, 1993, and after September 30, 1996. He is accordingly precluded from having his motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen.2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

Pursuant to 8 C.F.R.§§ 3.2(c) and 3.23(b)(3), a motion to reopen for the purpose of acting on an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation.

2

We therefore decline to address the issue of prima facie eligibility for relief from deportation, or whether the respondent would deserve a favorable exercise of discretion.

2003 WL 23508463 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TANIA MAXINE ROACH
File: AXX XX9 696 - Baltimore
November 20, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Oscar L. Amarow, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nelson A. Vargas-Padilla
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
Sec. 212(a)(2)(C), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(C)º - Controlled substance trafficker
 
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
 
Lodged: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) º - Controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s May 6, 2002, decision sustaining the charges of removability and pretermitting her application for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). The appeal will be dismissed in part and sustained in part, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
In her May 6, 2002, decision, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent failed to meet her burden of proving that she is entitled to be admitted to the United States and sustained the charges of removability against her. In addition, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent’s failure to timely submit an application for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act (Form I-191) constituted an abandonment of any such application. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erroneously placed upon her the burden of proving admissibility. Further, the respondent requests an opportunity to apply for section 212(c) relief and asserts that her failure to file such an application before the Immigration Judge was due to the ineffective assistance of her former counsel.
 
The respondent concedes that she is inadmissible and removable under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), for her violation of a state law relating to a controlled substance (Tr. at 24). As a returning lawful permanent resident who has “committed an offense identified in section 212(a)(2),” the respondent is properly classified as an applicant for admission. See section 101(a)(13)(C)(v) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(13)(C)(v). Accordingly, the Immigration Judge correctly concluded that she bears the burden of proving that she is “clearly and beyond doubt entitled to be admitted and is not inadmissible….” See section 240(c)(2) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(c)(2); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(b). The respondent’s appellate argument challenging the Immigration Judge’s allocation of the burden of proof is therefore without merit.
 
*2 Upon consideration of the Immigration Judge’s decision and the record as a whole, we are persuaded that the failure of the respondent’s former counsel to file a Form I-191 by the court imposed deadline constituted ineffective assistance of counsel on its face. It is apparent that the respondent had provided former counsel with all necessary documentation in support of the Form I-191 prior to the deadline and that former counsel’s failure to file by the deadline was the result of a mistaken assumption on her part that another attorney in the same law firm had already done so (Tr. at 31-33). In our view, an exercise of reasonable diligence would have revealed to former counsel the necessity of either filing the Form I-191 by the deadline or, at a minimum, timely requesting leave of court for an extension of the deadline. The Immigration Judge acknowledged that the respondent is prima facie eligible for a section 212(c) waiver pursuant to the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001), and therefore the failure of her former counsel to submit an application for such relief was highly prejudicial to the respondent’s interests. It appears that the respondent, though new counsel, is actively seeking to comply in good faith with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Therefore, we conclude that the preservation of the respondent’s due process right to a fair immigration hearing necessitates that the record be remanded to the Immigration Judge so that the respondent’s application for section 212(c) relief may be considered on the merits. We express no opinion at this time as to whether the respondent merits such relief in the exercise of discretion.
 
The following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed in part and sustained in part.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge so that the respondent’s request for a section 212(c) waiver may be considered on the merits.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Board Member Filppu respectfully dissents because the respondent has not shown that a bar complaint has been filed against prior counsel. Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996).

2003 WL 23508455 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUANGQUAN CHEN
File: AXX XX9 784 - Los Angeles
November 19, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patricia Vargas, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was rendered orally on November 19, 2002. The appeal was accordingly due on or before December 19, 2002. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on October 28, 2003. We accordingly find that the appeal is untimely. In conjunction with the appeal, the respondent filed a Motion for Leave of Court to File Untimely Notice of Appeal as a Result of Ineffective Assistance of Counsel, and Request for Certification in which the respondent is raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as the reason for the untimeliness of his appeal. We note that the respondent contends that a complaint was filed with the State Bar of California and that he forwarded a copy of his Motion for Leave of Court to File Untimely Notice of Appeal as a Result of Ineffective Assistance of Counsel, and Request for Certification to his prior counsel. However, the respondent did not provide any evidence to support this claim; and, therefore, he has failed to demonstrate prima facie eligibility for relief from removal. We recognize that in the Ninth Circuit a respondent need not strictly comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); but, at a minimum, the respondent must demonstrate that the alleged ineffective assistance had prejudiced him. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002). In this case, the respondent has failed to do so. Therefore, upon review of the record, we find we do not have a sufficient basis to consider this appeal by certification. The Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14 and 1240.15.
 
We note that because we have dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J- J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).

2003 WL 23508444 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ESTEBAN ARELLANO-MONTANO
File: AXX XX4 770 - Seattle
November 18, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael Johnson-Ortiz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Eric Bakken
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(G)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(G)(ii)º - Marriage fraud - failed or refused to fulfill marital agreement
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
  
APPLICATION: Remand.
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico. He appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 25, 2002, order graining voluntary departure upon his waiver of an appeal. He additionally requests that his case be remanded to the Immigration Judge so that he may pursue a self-petition under section 204(a)(1)(iii) of the Immigration ans Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1154(a)(1)(iii). We note that the record clearly indicates that the respondent affirmatively waived his right to appeal. See Tr. at 70. Thus, the appeal is generally barred, unless the respondent challenges the validity of the waiver. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(F); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39; Matter of L-V-K-, 22 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1999); Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001). We do possess jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s challenge to the validity of his appeal waiver. Id. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent avers, in his brief, that he did not knowingly and intelligently waive his right to appeal, but that he was coerced into waiving such right by the Immigration Judge. He avers that the Immigration Judge threatened him and committed multiple procedural errors. He further claims that the waiver of his right to appeal was not voluntary because he received ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Regarding ineffective assistance of counsel, the respondent must generally comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 900 (9th Cir. 2003). Here the respondent failed to submit an affidavit explaining his agreement with former counsel, failed to give his former counsel an opportunity to respond, and failed to submit proof, beyond the current attorney’s unsworn declaration, that he filed an attorney grievance. Id. Thus, we find that he failed to adequately comply with Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 Furthermore, we do not find that the respondent’s claims, regarding his former counsel’s behavior, establish that his former counsel’s assistance was ineffective, nor that such assistance resulted in prejudice to the respondent. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra. The respondent avers that his former attorney failed to timely file Form-I-751, a Petition to Remove Conditions on Residence. The respondent claims that his attorney made a representation that such petition was filed on August 9, 2000, when it was not actually filed on such date. However, the record reveals that the respondent’s former counsel merely indicated, on August 9, 2000, that the petition was completed, but was not yet ready to be filed because she did not have enough copies of certain documents. See Tr. at 15-16. The attorney later indicated that the I-751 had been filed in September of 2000. See Tr. at 19. Even if the attorney did not promptly file the respondent’s I-751, we do not find that a misrepresentation was made regarding filing, nor that any delay prejudiced the respondent’s case. The Immigration Judge did allow the respondent to file the I-751. See Tr. at 15-19.
 
The respondent further claims that his counsel was ineffective because she filed for a waiver of the requirement to file a joint petition after the commencement of removal proceedings, which the respondent avers is forbidden by the regulations. In contrast, we note that the regulations provide that a conditional resident who is in removal proceedings may apply for such waiver until such time as there is a final order of removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1216.5(a)(2). Thus, this does not constitute ineffective assistance.
 
The respondent’s current counsel also avers that the respondent’s former counsel failed to communicate the evidentiary requirements for a waiver under 8 C.F.R. § 1216.5. However, the respondent failed to present an affidavit concerning the agreement that he had with his former counsel, or explaining what was or was not said to him by his former counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra (discussing the Lozada requirements). Furthermore, the respondent did bring a file with documentation to support his request for a waiver, which was prepared by his former counsel. See Tr. at 43, 52-54. In fact, the respondent specifically testified that his former counsel had discussed the proof needed to support such a waiver. See Tr. at 54.
 
The respondent also claims that his former counsel failed to object, based on hearsay, to the admission of the form I-213. However, we note that this is typically an invalid objection. See Espinoza v. INS, 45 F.3d 308 (9th Cir. 1995) (finding that admission of a properly certified form I-213 is fair, despite the inability to cross-examine the preparer).
 
*3 The respondent additionally avers that his former counsel wrongfully withdrew from representing him in his proceedings. However, we note that the respondent’s former counsel had indicated that she was working on the case pro bono, she was having trouble communicating with the respondent as she did not speak Spanish, and the respondent had sent a letter to the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) indicating that she was not adequately representing him. See Tr. at 37-40, 42. Thus, we find that neither the respondent’s former counsel’s withdrawal, nor the Immigration Judge’s approval of such withdrawal, was inappropriate. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.17(b). In addition, the Immigration Judge did offer to allow the respondent a 2 week continuance to find a new attorney, but the respondent elected to proceed without an attorney. See Tr. at 41. In sum, we find that the respondent failed to establish that he received ineffective assistance of counsel or that this affected the voluntariness of his waiver of appeal.
 
The respondent additionally avers that the Immigration Judge’s conduct prevented him from knowingly and intelligently waiving his right to appeal. The respondent claims that the Immigration Judge inappropriately encouraged him to accept voluntary departure, prior to the conclusion of proceedings, rather than pursuing an appeal. Regarding the Immigration Judge’s conduct, we note that the respondent failed to establish, and does not aver, that he was eligible for voluntary departure, pursuant to section 240B(b) of the Act. Therefore, the respondent can not establish that the Immigration Judge’s representations, concerning the respondent’s option to appeal an oral decision or accept voluntary departure pursuant to section 240B(a) of the Act, were not accurate. Thus, the fact that the Immigration Judge explained to the respondent why he may prefer to elect voluntary departure does not establish that the Immigration Judge acted inappropriately, nor that the respondent was coerced in any way.
 
The Immigration Judge’s warning that the respondent’s current girlfriend, whom the respondent had testified was here illegally, could be discovered by the DHS was not incorrect. See Tr. at 70-71. Although the Immigration Judge may have mis spoke, when she indicated that the respondent could return illegally, we do not find that this error was prejudicial or merits a finding that the respondent’s waiver of an appeal was involuntary. See Tr. at 71.
 
Concerning additional actions of the Immigration Judge, the respondent avers that she inappropriately questioned him during his hearings. However, we note that the Immigration Judge has a duty to “interrogate, examine, and cross-examine” a respondent. See section 240(b)(1) of the Act. Furthermore, the respondent fails to specify any particular question that was inappropriate.
 
*4 The respondent further avers that the Immigration Judge inappropriately turned off the tape recorder, during the hearing, in order to coerce the respondent to accept voluntary departure. However, the respondent fails to reveal anything that was said by the Immigration Judge, while off the record, that was inappropriate. The respondent has not submitted an affidavit alleging what occurred off the record or why it was not appropriate. Thus, there is no basis for such a finding.
 
The respondent also avers that the Immigration Judge improperly permitted the DHS to amend the charge of removability, and that she failed to follow the requirements of 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(e). However, we note that the DHS is permitted to file additional charges, and the new charge was read to the respondent and he was served with a copy of the additional charge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(e); see also Tr. at 74. The respondent was also told that he could have a 2 week continuance to address the new charge, but he elected not to accept such a continuance. See Tr. at 74. In sum, we can not find that the Immigration Judge’s conduct resulted in an unfair hearing or that she inappropriately coerced the respondent’s waiver of appeal.
 
In addition, we can not find that the described actions, of the respondent’s former counsel or the Immigration Judge, have prejudiced the respondent’s case. The respondent seeks a remand to pursue relief pursuant to section 204(a)(1)(A)(iii) of the Act. The respondent avers that his former spouse physically and emotionally abused him. However, we note that the respondent previously testified that his wife did not hurt him, hit him, or beat him. See Tr. at 60-61. He also testified that she pushed him, but that he did not let her hit him. Id. He specifically stated “I never let her hit me.” Id. at 61. The respondent also testified that, although his former wife threatened to contact DHS, he did not consider that a threat because he was not afraid to return to his country. See Tr. at 62. When asked what hardship he would face if returned to Mexico, he said “there is no hardship.” See Tr. at 64. Thus, we can not find that the respondent’s case would have turned out differently if he had not waived appeal. Hence, even if the respondent had established ineffective assistance of counsel or that any of the Immigration Judge’s actions were inappropriate, we do not find that he has established prejudice to his case. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra, at 901.
 
Accordingly, we find that the respondent validly, knowingly, and intelligently waived his right to an appeal. In the alternative, we find that the respondent failed to establish that his case was prejudiced in any way. Thus, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2003 WL 23508436 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHAHANA AKHTER
File: AXX XX2 504 - New York
November 18, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Freddy Jacobs, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely appeal from an Immigration Judge’s March 10, 2003, decision denying her motion to reopen following a hearing held on October 4, 1995, in which she was ordered deported in absentia. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the respondent was personally served with an Order to Show Cause (OSC, Form 1-221) on July 6; 1995 (Exh. 5), and the OSC notified her to appear for a hearing on August 2, 1995. The respondent appeared pro se on August 2, 1995, and was given written notice to appear at a hearing on October 4, 1995. The respondent did not appear on that date and was ordered deported in absentia.
 
The respondent, through counsel, filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge on February 27, 2003, alleging that she did not receive the OSC, notice of her hearing, or the in absentia order, and alleging that her prior attorney had advised her that her case had been closed. In her motion, she also stated that she is now eligible for adjustment of status based on both an approved I-130 and an approved I-140. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, noting that the motion did not accurately recount what had happened, that the respondent had been personally served with notice of her October 4, 1995, hearing, and that the respondent therefore failed to establish lack of notice. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision.
 
On appeal, the respondent, through counsel, acknowledges that the facts as presented in her motion before the Immigration Judge were incorrect. In addition, she contends that her prior attorney advised her not to attend her October 4, 1995, hearing, and therefore she received ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent has not attempted to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), nor has she offered a reasonable explanation for her failure to do so at this time. Accordingly, on this record, the respondent has not established that her failure to appear at her hearing was due to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent on appeal also contends that she may not have received oral warnings of the consequences of failing to appear at her deportation hearing, and that there may not have been an interpreter present at her August 2, 1995, hearing when she received written notice. She therefore requests reopening to apply for adjustment of status, relief which was unavailable at the time of her hearing, pursuant to Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). However, the respondent’s motion to reopen for newly-acquired relief is untimely, as it was not filed within 90 days of the date of entry of her final administrative order, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. See Matter of M-S-, supra at 357 (finding that such motions are subject to the regulatory requirements of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)).
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

2003 WL 23521906 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MICHAEL CONROY TALBOT A.K.A. MICHAEL TALBOT
File: AXX XX6 502 - New York
November 14, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey Okun, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa M. Golub
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
Sec. 241(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on September 9, 2003, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reconsider. The respondent had sought reconsideration of the Immigration Judge’s March 14, 2003, denial of a motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered deported in absentia. Now, the respondent has filed a timely motion to reconsider dated October 2, 2003, in which he contends that the Board erred in dismissing his appeal. The motion is denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reconsider must be denied because it fails to specify any errors of fact or law in the Board’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (as renumbered by 68 Fed. Reg. 9824, 9830 (Feb. 28, 2003) (effective Feb. 28, 2003)). In the motion, the respondent contends that his appeal should have been considered by a panel of three Board Members. He argues that his original motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge should have been considered timely because his authorized representative paid for a timely motion to reopen; in the alternative, he argues that the filing deadline should have been equitably tolled due to the ineffective assistance of his counsel. The respondent also asserts that the Board should revisit its holding in Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335 (BIA 1991), which states that the Board and the Immigration Judges do not have the authority to apply the doctrine of equitable estoppel against the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) so as to preclude it from undertaking a lawful course of action that it is empowered to pursue by statute and regulation. Finally, the respondent asserts that he did have an attorney-client relationship with Michael Diraimondo, Esquire, and that he complied with the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
As we pointed out in our prior decision, even if the respondent could successfully make an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, he failed to demonstrate the due diligence required to equitably toll the filing deadline for the motion to reopen. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000) (finding that an alien who waited 2 years after the filing deadline failed to exercise due diligence for equitable tolling purposes). We also note that the fact that the respondent believes the appeal to have merit does not compel the Board to issue a decision by more than one Board Member. In general, the respondent’s arguments were either already addressed in the Board’s prior decision, or do not specify how the Board erred in making its determination. Therefore, the motion to reconsider must be denied.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2003 WL 23521897 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ELIJAH KARAGU KIHANYA
File: AXX XX3 898 - Baltimore
November 13, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Liam Ge, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Linda A. Dominguez
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(c)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
In a decision dated December 18, 2002, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen in order to apply for cancellation of removal. The respondent has appealed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
First, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen because, inter alia, the respondent failed to establish that the respondent’s wife would experience exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if he was removed from the United States. See Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001)(qualifying relative must experience hardship that is “‘substantially’ beyond the ordinary hardship that would be expected when a close family member leaves this country” in order to meet the “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” standard). A motion to reopen will not be granted unless the applicant establishes a prima facie case of eligibility for the underlying relief sought. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The Immigration Judge properly found that the respondent failed to comply with reopening standards.
 
Second, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) points out that the Immigration Judge granted the respondent the privilege of voluntary departure on October 28, 2002, but he failed to depart as required. The DHS contends that the respondent is thus not eligible for cancellation of removal. Pursuant to section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d), an alien who fails to depart following a grant of voluntary departure, and who has been provided written notice of the consequences of remaining in the United States, is statutorily barred from applying for certain forms of discretionary relief. We find that the respondent is barred from applying for cancellation of removal by section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d) because the he has remained in the United States after the scheduled date of departure.
 
The respondent also alleges that his prior counsel rendered ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has failed to comply with any of these requirements. See Stewart v. INS, 181 F.3d 587, 596 n. 9 (4th Cir. 1999). In the respondent’s affidavit, the respondent asserts that his former counsel is “bedridden and incommunicado,” and he thus could not notify him of his complaint. The respondent has submitted no evidence to substantiate these assertions. The respondent has also provided no reason why he could not have sent a letter outlining his complaint to his former counsel’s law office. Moreover, the respondent has not shown that he filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed

2003 WL 23521886 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SANG IL LEE
File: AXX XX5 642 - Seattle
November 12, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frederick P.S. Whang, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dana C. Laverty
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Remand; termination
 
In a decision dated May 10, 2000, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to suppress evidence and terminate proceedings, found him removable as charged, and granted him voluntary departure. The respondent has filed this appeal. The Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent was served with a Notice to Appear on February 11, 2000. It was alleged that he was a native and citizen of Korea who entered the United States as a nonimmigrant visitor on or about August 17, 1992, and was authorized to remain until September 16, 1992. He was charged with removability for remaining longer than permitted.
 
The respondent contested removability, and made a motion to suppress the evidence submitted by the DHS, and terminate proceedings. See generally Gonzalez-Rivera v. INS, 22 F.3d 1441 (9th Cir. 1994). The Immigration Judge’s decision sets out the respondent’s claim, and the evidence submitted by the respondent and the DHS (I.J. Dec. at 2-5). We adopt these findings on appeal, and will only summarize the facts here. See generally Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872 (BIA 1994).
 
The respondent was represented by Grosvenor Anschell at his hearing. The respondent testified that he was living in an apartment, and on February 11, 2000, an immigration agent and police officer knocked on the door around 7:00 a.m. The respondent opened the door. The officers identified themselves, asked him about his mother, and then walked inside (see Tr. at 20- 22, 27). The respondent did not give them permission to enter, and explained that he felt intimidated. The agents eventually asked the respondent about his immigration status, and he revealed that he was not lawfully present (Tr. at 20). On cross-examination, the respondent stated that the officers did not threaten him when they were at the door, that he never asked them to leave, that they never touched him or showed weapons, and that they allowed him to move around the apartment and use the telephone (see Tr. at 27-34).
 
The DHS presented the testimony of the immigration agent and the police officer who went to the respondent’s apartment on the morning in question. The immigration officer testified that they went to look for the respondent’s mother, who was suspected of being involved in a smuggling scheme. An unknown female answered the door, and gave them permission to enter. They subsequently encountered the respondent, who admitted that he was illegally present in the United States.
 
*2 The police officer testified that they were allowed in the apartment by the female, and that the respondent was told that he was not under arrest, and he freely answered questions. Both officers stated that if they are not invited into a residence they would leave (Tr. at 54, 73-74).
 
The Immigration Judge found that the officers testified credibly about the events in question. He noted that their testimonies were consistent, and that they were acting in their capacity as law enforcement officers. He did not find that the respondent’s testimony successfully rebutted their version of events. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to suppress, found that the Record of Deportable Alien (Form I-213) (Exh. 2) established the respondent’s removability, and granted him voluntary departure.
 
On review of the record, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent did not establish a basis for suppressing the evidence in question, and was removable as charged. We therefore adopt and affirm this portion of his decision. See Matter of Burbano, supra. We now address the respondent’s arguments on appeal.
 
The respondent now has new counsel, and he asserts that he received ineffective assistance of counsel at his hearing. Specifically, his attorney was not prepared, did not produce a key witness, and conducted cursory and ineffective examinations of the respondent and the DHS’ witnesses. The respondent submitted supporting documentation in accordance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); but see Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003) (ineffective assistance of counsel may be established regardless of compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra). He also provided proof that Mr. Anschell was suspended for 2 years by the Washington state Bar Association on October 30, 2000, due in part to faulty representation of his clients.1
 
Even assuming that the respondent has established that his prior counsel was ineffective, we conclude that he has not established that he was prejudiced by this conduct. See Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (BIA 1999) (internal cites omitted). Specifically, we have no firm indication of what other evidence the respondent would have provided, and how it would have changed the outcome of his case. Rather, the respondent only makes general assertions that his case could have been better prepared and presented, and he speculates as to alternative outcomes.
 
For example, the respondent claims that a parent-child privilege may have been asserted which would have precluded some of his testimony at the hearing. However, he does not explain the origin of this “privilege” or how he was prejudiced by any admissions related to his parents. Similarly, the respondent asserts that a better attorney would have called the female who answered the door to testify, as this might have impeached the officer’s testimony. However, we have no statement from the female, or even the respondent, indicating that this witness would have been available for testimony, or would halve provided beneficial information. The respondent also states that a more effective cross-examination might have helped his case. However, his claim in this area is speculative, and is not supported by specific examples of why further cross-examination would have changed the result in this case. In this regard, we point out that the officers’ did present comprehensive testimony about the events in question, although most was revealed in the course of direct examination rather than cross-examination.2
 
*3 Any potential claim of prejudice is also undermined by the fact that even if the motion to suppress was granted, there would still be an alternative basis for removability. We refer to the case law establishing that even where an illegal arrest or search has occurred, the gathering of independent evidence based on the discovery of a person’s identity need not be excluded, and this includes information in DHS files and admissions made at a hearing. See Matter of Cervantes-Torres, 21 I&N Dec. 351, 353 (BIA 1996) (citing United States v. Guzman-Bruno, 27 F.3d 420 (9th Cir. 1994), cert. denied, 513 U.S. 796 (1984); Rodriguez-Gonzalez v. INS, 640 F.2d 1139 (9th Cir. 1981), cert denied, 513 U.S. 975 (1994) (and other cases)). In this case, the DHS obtained independent records establishing that the respondent has overstayed his permitted time in this country (see Exh. 2, Form I-213 and DHS printouts). The respondent’s removability therefore could have been established even assuming that the motion to suppress should have been granted.3
 
On appeal, the respondent also alleges that the DHS precluded him from presenting evidence on his behalf because they did not arrest the woman who answered the apartment door on the day in question. The respondent’s reasoning is that if the woman had been investigated, she would have been more likely to testify on the respondent’s behalf. This argument fails for several reasons. First, a decision by the DHS to institute removal proceedings involves the exercise of prosecutorial discretion and is not a decision that the Immigration Judge or this Board may review. See Matter of G-N-C-, 22 I&N Dec. 281 (BIA 1998). Second, it is the respondent’s responsibility to produce evidence to support his case, and if he wanted this witness to testify, the burden was on him to have made appropriate efforts. We see no reason to address the issue further.
 
We therefore find no basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision based on the arguments made on appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*4 NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

2

On appeal, the DHS challenges the respondent’s portrayal of events at the hearing. We agree that there is some inaccuracy. For example, the appeal states that the DHS attorney told the respondent he could not consult with his counsel (see Appeal Brief at 6). However, the statements in question involve the Immigration Judge instructing the respondent that he should not confer with counsel before answering questions on cross-examination unless there was a legal concern (see Tr. at 23).

3

We are therefore not persuaded by the respondent’s attempts on appeal to distinguish his case from Rodriguez-Gonzalez v. INS, supra (see Appeal Brief at 9).

2003 WL 23521864 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OSAZUWA MONDY IYAMU A.K.A. MOHAMMED MUSTAPHA MOMODU
File: AXX XX6 603 - San Francisco
November 6, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jesse A. Lloyd, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James A. Lazarus
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on July 31, 2002, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The Immigration Judge pretermitted the respondent’s application for adjustment of status because it was incomplete and lacked an immediately available visa. The Immigration Judge also denied the respondent’s application for withholding of removal based on his failure to make a claim. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen proceedings based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the respondent’s motion on the basis that the respondent failed to provide a legally persuasive justification for tolling the 90-day statute of limitations. The respondent’s motion will be granted.
 
In his motion, the respondent alleges that he suffered a due process violation as a result of his prior counsel’s ineffective assistance. In particular, the respondent contends that his prior counsel should have pursued his claim for asylum and withholding of removal.
 
We find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).1 Therefore, we turn to the issue of whether the respondent has shown that he suffered prejudice as a result of his counsel’s ineffective assistance. Prejudice results when “the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” See Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999).
 
The respondent contends that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s failure to pursue his claim for asylum. The respondent’s prior counsel, contends that he did not do so because the respondent was statutorily barred as a result of the 1-year filing deadline. The respondent entered in June 1995, and was served with the Notice to Appear on March 4, 1999. Therefore, the respondent would have had to file for asylum by April 1, 1998. See 8 C.F.R. § 1208.4(a)(2)(ii) (providing that the 1-year filing deadline shall be calculated from the date of the alien’s last arrival in the United States or April 1, 1997, whichever is later). The respondent contends that his reliance on his pending application for adjustment of status qualifies as an extraordinary circumstance permitting a late-filed application for asylum. The respondent claims that his wife filed a visa petition on his behalf in March 1997 (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 8). We do not find that filing of a visa petition constitutes an extraordinary circumstance because such a filing does not guarantee approval and adjustment of status. Therefore, inasmuch as the respondent was statutorily ineligible for asylum due to the 1-year filing deadline, we find that the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by his prior attorney’s conduct. See section 208(a)(2)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158(a)(2)(B).
 
*2 The respondent’s prior counsel contends that he did not pursue his claim for withholding of removal because the respondent was ineligible for relief based on the DHS’s finding that the respondent engaged in marriage fraud. Initially, we note that the Immigration Judge did not make a finding on the issue of marriage fraud. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for adjustment of status because it was incomplete and lacked an immediately available visa. Furthermore, marriage fraud does not render the respondent ineligible for withholding of removal. See section 241(b)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(b)(3). We are also troubled by the prior counsel’s submission of a Form I-589 which does not answer the questions related to persecution (Exh. 2). Moreover, the respondent appears to be prima facie eligible for such relief based on his newly filed Form I-589 and supporting documentation, which were submitted with his motion to reopen. Based on the foregoing, we find that the respondent has established that he was prejudiced by his prior counsel’s ineffective assistance. See Ortiz v. INS, supra.
 
The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the circuit in which this case arises, has held that the statute of limitations for filing a motion to reopen is tolled from the time that an alien has been victimized by a negligent attorney until he learns of the negligence. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000). The respondent contends that he discovered his prior counsel’s ineffectiveness in a meeting with his current counsel’s on June 16, 2003. The respondent filed his motion to reopen on July 25, 2003. Therefore, based on binding circuit law, we will equitably toll the 90-day deadline for filing motions to reopen. Id. Accordingly, we find that the respondent’s motion was timely filed.
 
Based on the foregoing, we will grant the motion to reopen and remand proceedings to the Immigration Judge for a hearing on the respondent’s application for withholding of removal.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for entry of a new decision.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

The DHS does not dispute that the respondent has met the procedural requirements under Matter of Lozada, supra.


2003 WL 23521864 (BIA)

2003 WL 23521862 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMANAND BHULAI
File: AXX-XX3-574 - Miami
November 6, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hector M. Diaz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF SERVICE:
 
Georgina M. Picos
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. By order dated July 7, 2003, the Immigration Judge sustained the charge of removability against the respondent, denied his request for relief from removal, and ordered him removed. The Immigration Judge’s order reflects that the respondent, who was represented by counsel, waived his right to appeal. On July 10, 2003, however, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider in which he requested that the Immigration Judge vacate that portion of his July 7, 2003, decision finding that the respondent had waived his appeal rights. The Immigration Judge denied the motion on the grounds that his prior decision was legally and factually correct and that the respondent’s appeal waiver appeared to have been knowingly and intelligently made.
 
Whenever the right to appeal is waived, the decision of the Immigration Judge becomes final and may be implemented immediately. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.39. Moreover, by waiving appeal, an alien relinquishes the opportunity to obtain review of the Immigration Judge’s ruling. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(a)(1). Thus, it is important that any appeal waiver be knowingly and intelligently made. See United States v. Mendoza-Lopez, 481 U.S. 828, 840 (1987); see also Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320 (BIA 2000). In fact, immigration judges are obliged by statute to notify aliens subject to removal of their appeal rights. See section 240(c)(4) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4). A party wishing to challenge the validity of an appeal waiver may file either a motion to reconsider with the Immigration Judge or a direct appeal with this Board. See Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001). In this instance, the respondent chose the former route. To be valid, a motion to reconsider before the Immigration Judge must specify the legal or factual errors in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision and must be supported by pertinent authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). In this instance, however, the respondent’s motion to reconsider did not allege that the Immigration Judge committed any legal or factual error, such as failing to comply with the notification requirements of section 240(c)(4) of the Act. Rather, the respondent alleges that he did not understand the consequences of his failure to seek an appeal. In as much as the respondent alleges ineffective assistance of counsel, he has failed to satisfy any of the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
*2 While the respondent may in retrospect regret his decision to waive appeal, we see no basis for disturbing the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2003 WL 23521862 (BIA)

2003 WL 23521846 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JULIA CACERES-GUAMAN
File: AXX XX5 348 - Newark
November 5, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pascal W. Stingone, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the decision dated August 28, 2002, in which an Immigration Judge denied, the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. On July 9, 1998, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent voluntary departure. On August 2, 2002, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, claiming ineffective assistance of counsel. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the failure of her former attorney to file a timely motion to reopen to apply for adjustment of status constituted ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also argues that equitable tolling should be applied. However, we need not reach this issue since the respondent has failed to comply with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).1 See Stojkovic v. INS, 54 Fed.Appx. 728, 2002 WL 31546506 (3rd Cir. 2002) (claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was not exempt from the Lozada procedure); Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127 (3rd Cir. 2001) (alien did not satisfy the procedural requirements of Lozada).
 
Furthermore, the record indicates that at the conclusion of immigration proceedings on July 9, 1998, the respondent was granted voluntary departure until November 16, 1998. The respondent was provided oral and written warnings of the consequences of remaining in the United States after the termination of the period of voluntary departure. The record further indicates that the respondent waived appeal of that decision. Pursuant to section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d), an alien who fails to depart following a grant of voluntary departure, and who has been provided written notice of the consequences of remaining in the United States, is statutorily barred from applying for certain forms of discretionary relief. We find that the respondent is barred from applying for adjustment of status by section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d). An alien who remains in the United States after the scheduled date of departure is statutorily ineligible for discretionary relief. Therefore, because the respondent has remained in the United States after the scheduled date of departure, the respondent is statutorily ineligible for the relief sought. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

The respondent did not submit an affidavit which sets forth the agreement between the respondent and her former attorney; the respondent did not submit any evidence that her former counsel was informed of the allegations leveled against him; and the respondent failed to state why a bar complaint was not filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding her former attorney’s conduct.

2003 WL 23521856 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SOON JA WANG CHOI
File: AXX XX2 977 - Honolulu
November 5, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Dennis W. Jung, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This is the third time that the respondent’s case has been before the Board. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on October 31, 2002. We denied a motion to reopen on April 24, 2003, and denied another motion to reopen on July 23, 2003. The respondent filed yet another motion to reopen on September 8, 2003. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent brings a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent claims that counsel representing her on January 24, 2003, filed a “frivolous motion to reopen”, seeking adjustment of status based on marriage to a United States citizen (Respondent’s Mot., at 8). The respondent argues that, since the I-130 visa petition had not been approved at that time, that former counsel erred in filing the motion to reopen.
 
Even were the motion deadline and numerical limitations for motions excused, the respondent fails to show prejudice resulting from her attorney’s actions. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226 (9th Cir. 2002); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000). The respondent’s January 24, 2003, motion was not “frivolous”. Rather, in Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002), the Board held that a properly filed motion to reopen for adjustment of status based on a marriage entered into after the commencement of proceedings may be granted in the exercise of discretion, notwithstanding the pendency of a visa petition filed on the alien’s behalf, under certain circumstances.
 
In our April 24, 2003, decision denying reopening, we mistakenly stated that the motion could not be granted because the respondent failed to submit proof of an approved visa petition. However, we went on to say that a favorable exercise of discretion was not warranted. As noted, the decision to reopen proceedings involves the exercise of discretion. Matter of Velarde, supra. The record includes the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent “is engaged in prostitution”, which she based on testimony of a Honolulu police officer concerning “various sexual acts” which the respondent had offered to perform for payment (I.J. at 4-8). The respondent fails to show how former counsel caused us to exercise discretion unfavorably. Instead, the respondent’s own behavior caused that result.
 
*2 The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Singh v. Gonzales, 9th Cir., September 6, 2007
2003 WL 23521835 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KULWANT SINGH
File: AXX XX9 285 - San Francisco
November 3, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Earle A. Sylva, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jayme Salinardi
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on July 29, 2002. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before October 28, 2002. The record reflects, however, that the respondent first submitted the motion on August 18, 2003, but it was rejected and not actually filed until September 2, 2003. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time. The respondent has requested sua sponte acceptance of the motion. The Board retains limited discretionary powers under the regulations to reopen or reconsider cases on our own motion. 8 C.F.R § 1003.2(a). That power, however, allows the Board to reopen proceedings sua sponte in exceptional situations which we find are not present here. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
In his motion, the respondent has raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, the motion must establish prejudice arising from the alleged failings of prior counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent here has met these requirements, with the exception of the third. However, his attorney has responded to the allegations against him and established that he attempted to notify the respondent of the Board’s prior decision.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied. As we have denied the motion to reopen, the motion for a stay of deportation is also denied.

2003 WL 23521810 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MORAUND FELIX
File: AXX XX3 179 - Orlando
October 31, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On January 24, 2000 the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal for lack of jurisdiction, because it was untimely filed We accordingly returned the record to the Immigration Court without further action. On February 15, 2001, the respondent has submitted a Form EOIR-26, Notice of Appeal to the Board of Immigration Appeals from a Decision of an INS Officer. Due to its content, it will be construed as a motion to reopen. On May 3, 2002, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider. These motions will be denied.
 
With respect to respondent’s motion to reopen, filed on February 15, 2001, the Board does not normally have jurisdiction over motions filed in cases where the Board never assumed initial jurisdiction, such as when an alien waives his appeal rights. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974). We retain jurisdiction over the instant motion to the extent that it challenges the finding of untimeliness or requests consideration of the reasons for untimeliness. See Matter of Lopez, Interim Decision 3343 (BIA 1998).
 
With respect to the motion to reconsider, a motion to reconsider in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 30 days after the date of that decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). In the instant case, a motion to reconsider would have been due on or before February 23, 2000. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive respondent’s motion to reconsider until May 3, 2002. The motion to reconsider was therefore filed out of time.
 
In his motion, the respondent has raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, the motion must establish prejudice arising from the alleged failings of prior counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent here has failed to meet these requirements.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.

2003 WL 23270101 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZI TSUEN ZHOU
File: A78 258 106 - San Diego
October 28, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michael A. O. Brown, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent seeks reopening and rescission of his in absentia order based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), “not sacrosanct,” and substantial compliance may be sufficient). However, the ineffective assistance that has been alleged by the respondent relates to former counsels’ failure to prevail on a motion to change venue in time for him to make travel arrangements. He has not alleged that ineffective assistance of counsel precluded him from filing the motion to reopen within the statutorily required period.
 
There is no dispute that the respondent received proper notice of the hearing. In fact, according to the respondent, he was personally aware of the hearing date and that his counsel had not been successful in securing a grant of a change of venue. Unless lack of notice has been demonstrated, a motion to rescind an in absentia order must be filed within 180 days of the in absentia order. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The in absentia order was entered on February 22, 2001. His motion was not filed until July 9, 2002. The respondent’s motion is untimely, and there is no equitable tolling issue on these facts. We therefore need not reach the question whether there was ineffective assistance of counsel relating to the motion to change venue that would constitute an exceptional circumstance for his failure to appear.1
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We point out, however, that the submission of a motion to change venue does not generally relieve an alien of his obligation to attend the scheduled hearing absent notification that the motion had been granted. See Hernandez-Vivas v. INS, 23 F.3d 1557 (9th Cir. 1994).
