2003 WL 23270145 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LETICIA PEREZ-URIOSTEGUI
File: AXX XX5 549 - Chicago
October 28, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Claudia Valenzuela, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Karen E. Lundgren,
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 On May 6, 2003, an Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged, he deemed her application for adjustment of status to be abandoned, and he ordered her removal to Mexico. The respondent appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be sustained.
 
We note that, at the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the respondent stood convicted of a felony in the State of Illinois. On June 16, 2003, however, that conviction was vacated, and the respondent was convicted of misdemeanor theft.
 
The Immigration Judge considered the respondent’s application abandoned because of her failure to file proper documentation in a timely manner, and his decision appears to have been well within his authority and discretion. However, certain facts have been presented subsequent to the Immigration Judge’s decision, after jurisdiction had passed from the Immigration Judge to the Board, that persuade us that the respondent likely was prejudiced by her former counsel’s conduct of her case.
 
Litigants are generally bound by the conduct of their attorneys, absent egregious circumstances. LeBlanc v. INS, 715 F.2d 685 (1st Cir. 1983): Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In statements by the respondent and her aunt, statements which were signed but not notarized as affidavits, it is reported that she had significant difficulty both communicating with her former counsel and understanding what he was doing in regard to her case. The respondent stated that she has been detained throughout these proceedings and, while the attorney who was involved in her criminal proceedings had been cooperative, former counsel in her removal proceedings had not been. It is alleged that the respondent was “confused” on the day of the hearing and could not understand why she was ordered removed, rather than allowed to pursue her adjustment application. After it became apparent that former counsel was not being “straightforward” with her, after filing an appeal form, the respondent reportedly discharged him.
 
The respondent’s current counsel reports that former counsel has been uncooperative in providing access to her file, further complicating her appeal, and a complaint has been filed with his state bar disciplinary board. According to current counsel, former counsel has been advised of the action taken but has not responded.
 
It appears to us that the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, in demonstrating ineffective assistance of counsel, to include providing former counsel the opportunity to respond to her allegations. We note that former counsel was reprimanded on May 20, 2003, by the Attorney Registration and Disciplinary Commission of the Supreme Court of Illinois. While that reprimand clearly was not related to the matter at hand, it does serve to demonstrate that former counsel does have a history of engaging in misconduct. Of particular concern to us is former counsel’s apparent refusal to allow the respondent access to her file, which, according to current counsel, constitutes a violation of Rule 1.15 of the Illinois Rules of Professional Conduct.
 
*2 While we certainly support the Immigration Judge’s requirement that applications for relief from removal be timely filed, and we find no error on his part, considering the facts available to him at the time of his decision, we are of the opinion that, in this case, the respondent’s opportunity to apply for a relief for which she has at least a colorable claim was denied due to an egregious inaction on the part of her former counsel. We will consequently sustain her appeal and remand this case to afford her the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status and a corresponding waiver of inadmissibility.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and the Immigration Judge’s decision is vacated insofar as it relates to the finding that the respondent abandoned her application for adjustment and a waiver of inadmissibility.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and a new decision.

FOR THE BOARD

2003 WL 23270091 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LI, RONG JIAO*
File: AXX-XX5-835 - NEW YORK
October 27, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Day, Charles, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). We will generally defer to an Immigration Judge’s adverse credibility finding that is based on inconsistencies and omissions that are central to the claim where (1) the discrepancies and omissions are actually present in the record; (2) the discrepancies and omissions provide specific and cogent reasons for the adverse credibility finding; and (3) the alien has not provided a convincing explanation for the discrepancies and omissions. Matter of S-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 1328 (BIA 2000); Matter of A-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 1106 (BIA 1998); see also Secaida-Rosales v. INS, 331 F.3d 297 (2d Cir. 2003) (holding that an Immigration Judge must provide specific, cogent reasons for rejecting an alien’s testimony; those reasons must bear a legitimate nexus to the finding, and must be valid grounds for disregarding the testimony). In addition, the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit has held that the presence or absence of corroborating evidence may properly be considered in assessing an asylum applicant’s credibility where it could reasonably be expected. See Diallo v. INS, 232 F.3d 279 (2d Cir. 2000). In addition to the discrepancies and omission noted by the Immigration Judge, the respondent failed to provide any corroborating information from her father, mother, or other students with which she allegedly practiced Falun Gong. Despite the generous amount of time that the respondent had to prepare her claim, she failed to acquire any of these easily obtainable and verifiable documents. Matter of S-M-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 722, 725 (BIA 1997). With respect to the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, she has not complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2003 WL 23270061 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEONARDA ALONSO
File: A71 593 114 - Los Angeles
October 22, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carlos Cruz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lee P. Crystal
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)] - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on January 7, 2003, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s denial of suspension of deportation. The respondent, who is a native and citizen of Mexico, now alleges that she was ineffectively represented by her former counsel, Peter DeBruyn.1 She requests that we reopen proceedings and remand to the Immigration Court so that she may submit additional information. The motion is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2.
 
Our previous decision upheld the Immigration Judge because the respondent failed to demonstrate good moral character as required for suspension of deportation under former section 244(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1254(a)(1). In particular, the respondent had failed to submit a required police “clearance,” even though she had admittedly been jailed in the past (Tr. at 26-27). The respondent now alleges, however, that it was Mr. DeBruyn who should have made her aware of the requirement. She essentially argues that this attorney’s alleged failure to advise her of this excused her from providing the information. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).2
 
As an initial matter, we observe that the respondent could have made her Lozada-related claim prior to our last decision since she was already represented at that time by a new attorney. Her original appellate brief is bereft of any such arguments; she has offered no reason why she failed to raise such contentions sooner or why she should be permitted a second chance to do so now.
 
In any event, the respondent has still failed to submit any type of police clearance; she now merely alleges in her motion that she would do so if the case were remanded. This is noteworthy since the respondent has now had nearly 5 years to provide such evidence, but has not done so even though she has retained at least two additional attorneys since she was before the Immigration Court. We are not inclined to find that the attorney who represented her previously provided her with ineffective representation since it appears that the respondent herself has refused to submit such evidence. A motion to reopen will not be granted if an alien fails to establish prima facie eligibility for the underlying relief sought. See Melkonian v. Ashcroft, 320 F.3d 1061, 1072 (9th Cir. 2003)(ineffective assistance of counsel context); see also INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). We find that the respondent has still failed to provide prima facie evidence of good moral character. We also observe that the respondent testified that her suspension application was true (Tr. at 18-19) yet therein she apparently falsely denied ever having been incarcerated. Compare Tr. at 26-27 with Exh. 2 (Form EOIR-40), Item 55. Thus, we would deny the motion to reopen for that reason as well. Cf. INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94, 105-106 (1988) (in cases involving discretionary relief, the Board may deny a motion to reopen solely in the exercise of discretion, even where prima facie eligibility for such relief has been shown). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
*2 The respondent contends that the former attorney in question did not properly assist her with respect to establishing the 7 years continuous physical presence required for suspension eligibility. See generally, Matter of Nolasco, 22 I&N Dec. 632 (BIA 1999). A review of the record, however, reveals that the respondent did attempt to establish physical presence, but that her own evidence fell short. Why she blames her prior attorney for this deficiency is not clear, nor does she offer an explanation as to why she failed to make this Lozada argument during her original appeal Again, even with her motion to reopen, she has still not submitted any additional evidence that would properly establish the required physical presence. See Melkonian v. Ashcroft, supra. Accordingly, we do not find that her former counsel provided ineffective assistance to her in this regard. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F. 3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (stating that Matter of Lozada, supra, “is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one”).
 
Similarly, the respondent now avers that her former attorney prejudiced her claim by failing to appear at her final merits hearing before the Immigration Judge. However, she was represented by another attorney at that time, and she has not submitted persuasive evidence to show that the result would have differed. Moreover, she did not raise this issue either when her case was on direct appeal to this Board. She has offered no reason why she should now have a second chance to raise new objections to the proceedings below.
 
In light of the foregoing, we find that the respondent has failed to identify any shortcoming in her prior representation which would lead us to reopen proceedings pursuant to Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
Finally, we note that in an attempt to show that she would experience extreme hardship upon deportation, another requirement for suspension relief, the respondent has now submitted several recent medical records with her motion detailing various disabilities—most notably regarding a knee problem.3 According to the records, the respondent underwent a total left knee replacement. She subsequently had soft tissue swelling and a 10- to 15-percent diminished range of motion with occasional to intermittent moderate pain. We observe that while these records were created after the Immigration Judge’s decision, essentially all of them, except for one physician’s letter which simply lists her various diagnoses, predate the Board’s decision. Thus these records could have previously been submitted, and we would have had to consider such evidence prior to our decision. See Ramirez-Alejandre v. Ashcroft, 319 F.3d 365 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Kazemi, 19 I&N Dec. 49 (BIA 1984). The respondent has offered no persuasive reason why proceedings should be reopened to permit her another opportunity to submit such evidence that she should have already had in her possession before the Board’s decision. Further, none of this information amounts to prima facie evidence that the respondent would experience extreme hardship upon return to her country.4 In her motion, the respondent’s current attorney concedes that, “arguably…[the respondent] could seek medical attention in Mexico to treat this array of problems ….” Respondent’s Motion at 6. But, it is contended, she would likely be unable to meet the financial cost of such treatment. This argument is insufficient because it is no more than unsupported speculation. The respondent has submitted no evidence concerning the cost of relevant medical treatment in Mexico. She doubts her ability to obtain “semi-sedentary” work, a limitation imposed by her medical condition, but has offered no evidence that such work is unavailable to her in Mexico. Thus, her belated attempts to establish extreme hardship do not persuade us to reopen proceedings.
 
*3 The respondent has thus failed to demonstrate prima facie eligibility for suspension of deportation and thus offers us no persuasive reason why proceedings should be reopened. See Melkonian v. Ashcroft, supra. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We find that it is thus unnecessary to further address the Lozada requirements. See e.g., Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237, 1240, n.6 (9th Cir. 2000) (failure to comply with Lozada requirements is not necessarily fatal to a motion to reopen).

2003 WL 23270179 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HARRIS KRISTANTO SARENGAT NG
File: AXX XX1 715 - Memphis
October 20, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Camille O. Bowman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Harris Lee Leatherwood
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on August 28, 2002. The motion was filed late, on June 10, 2003. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion.
 
Underlying the motion is a claim of ineffective assistance of the respondent’s former attorney. There is, however, no excuse provided for the lateness of the motion. The respondent was unrepresented by counsel on appeal, and our decision was promptly received by him (Respondent’s Affidavit).
 
In any event, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 191 & N Dec. 637 (BIA1988). These cases require that the alien file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority, or adequately explain why no filing was made. The motion states that the respondent “intends” to file a complaint against counsel, but does not say when the respondent might do so (Respondent’s Mot., at 6). This does not evidence compliance with the requirements of our case law. See Hamid v. Ashcroft, 336 F.3d 465, 469 (6th Cir. 2003) (“The fact that a grievance will be filed, without more, does not satisfy Lozada’s requirement that a complaint be filed before the affidavit is submitted to the BIA, nor does it furnish an explanation of why it was not so filed.”) (emphasis in original).
 
The Board in Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599, 603-605 (BIA 1996), gave several reasons for the “bar complaint” requirement. We noted that the requirement protects against collusion between aliens and counsel in which “ineffective assistance” is used to achieve delay. Id. We found that the bar complaint, like the other filings required by Matter of Lozada, supra, greatly aids us in adjudicating the claim. Id. We also stated that the filing of such a complaint is a “relatively small inconvenience” for an alien seeking a new hearing after proceedings have been concluded at great government expense. Id. The disciplinary process could consider, and evaluate, the actions taken by former counsel. Matter of Rivera-Claros, supra. The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2003 WL 23269968 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RABAH A. ABUSABE
File: A35 934 280 - Miami
October 17, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George J. DeFabio, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF SERVICE:
 
Frieda M. Goldstein
Assistant District Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
On appeal, the respondent contends that the Immigration Judge erred by denying his motion to reopen, and asserts that he established ineffective assistance of counsel and complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, l9 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (lst Cir. 1988).
 
In his decision dated September 9, 2002, the Immigration Judge noted that during the proceedings on June 13, 2002, the respondent was instructed to file all applications for relief by August 12, 2002. The record reflects that on August 12, 2002, the respondent’s former attorney filed a motion to withdraw as the respondent’s attorney of record, indicating that the respondent had ignored her repeated requests to set up an appointment at her office to complete the applications due by August 12, 2002. With her motion to withdraw, the respondent’s former attorney submitted copies of letters that she sent to the respondent dated June 17, 2002, July 18, 2002, and August 7, 2002. In the first two letters the respondent’s former attorney advised the respondent that he must communicate with her office and make an appointment to prepare the applications. In the letter dated August 7, 2002, she informed the respondent that due to his lack of cooperation, she would be withdrawing as his attorney of record. However, the respondent failed to submit any applications for relief by the deadline of August 12, 2002, as required. Consequently, the Immigration Judge deemed the respondent’s applications abandoned for failure to file.
 
A failure to request relief in accordance with a schedule set by the Immigration Judge constitutes a waiver of the opportunity to file for such relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (2003) (stating that if an application is not filed within the time limit set by the Immigration Judge, the opportunity to file that application shall be deemed abandoned). The Board has long held that applications for benefits under the Act are properly denied as abandoned when the alien fails to timely file them. See Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547, 549 (BIA 1992); Matter of Jean, 17 I&N Dec. 100, 102 (BIA 1979). The respondent was given two months to submit any applications for relief, which he failed to do. We find that the Immigration Judge provided the respondent with sufficient time to complete any applications applications for relief. Thus the respondent’s failure to file the applications for relief resulted in a waiver of his opportunity to file for such relief. Furthermore, the respondent failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel. He has not complied with all of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, and the letters submitted by his former attorney of record clearly conflict with the respondent’s claims of ineffective assistance of counsel.1 Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

However, the respondent has not submitted any telephone records verifying that such telephone calls were made by his brother and brother-in-law to his former attorney of record between June 13, 2002, and August 12, 2002.

2003 WL 23269884 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: REYES MARIN-MONTOYA
File: A42 325 982 - Dallas
October 17, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antonio A. Lopez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Saundra Haynes Arrington
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On December 18, 2001, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal for lack of jurisdiction, because it was untimely filed. We accordingly returned the record to the Immigration Court without further action. Now the respondent has filed a motion with the Board requesting that we reconsider our dismissal of the untimely appeal. However, the motion to reconsider has been filed out of time and will be denied.
 
The Board does not normally have jurisdiction over motions filed in cases where the Board never assumed initial jurisdiction, such as an untimely appeal. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974). We retain jurisdiction over the instant motion to the extent that it challenges the finding of untimeliness or requests consideration of the reasons for untimeliness. See Matter of Lopez, Interim Decision 3343 (BIA 1998).
 
A motion to reconsider in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 30 days after the date of that decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). In the instant case, a motion to reconsider would have been due on or before January 17, 2002. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until May 22, 2003. The motion to reconsider was therefore filed out of time.
 
In his motion, the respondent has raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, the motion must establish prejudice arising from the alleged failings of prior counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent here has failed to meet these requirements. While a copy of the complaint filed against his former attorney with the Texas Bar Association has been included with the motion,1 the respondent has not included a copy of an affidavit setting forth the agreement entered into with former counsel; nor is there anything in the record to indicate that the respondent has informed his former attorney of the allegations lodged against him.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

It is noted that the complaint filed with the Texas Bar Association states respondent hired his previous attorney to represent him in a misdemeanor assault case, and that there was no contract signed in the matter (see Part B: Information About Attorney of respondent’s grievance form).

IN RE: HARVINDER SINGH
File: A73 050 208 - San Francisco
October 7, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Martin Resendez Guajardo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lisa M. Koven
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of India, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered deported in absentia on June 14, 2001, under section 240(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b). The appeal is dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent, through counsel, argues that although notice of the hearing was purportedly sent to his former attorney, he never received the notice or knew of the hearing date. The respondent has attached, to his brief on appeal, records from his file that he obtained through a Freedom of Information Act request. These include transcripts from the master calendar hearing on May 21, 1999, and the hearing from which he was absent, and a letter to his former attorney requesting written proof that she had attempted to contact him regarding his hearing date.
 
We find that proper notice of the June 14, 2001, hearing, was provided to the respondent.1 The record shows that his former attorney, Ms. Alison Dixon, Esquire, was acting as his representative because she entered a Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative (Form EOIR-28) in the record of proceeding. The record also shows that Ms. Dixon was provided with a copy of the hearing notice by regular mail and she appeared on the appointed time for the hearing. See Exh. 4; Resp. Appeal Br. at B (transcript). Notice to counsel constitutes notice to the respondent. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2) (as renumbered by 68 Fed. Reg. 9824, 9830 (Feb. 28, 2003) (effective Feb. 28, 2003)); see also Garcia v. INS, 222 F.3d 1208, 1209 (9th Cir. 2000); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (as renumbered by 68 Fed. Reg. 9824, 9845 (Feb. 28, 2003) (effective Feb. 28, 2003)). If proper notice was provided to the respondent’s counsel of record, the in absentia order may not be rescinded unless the respondent prevails on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in accordance with the requirements as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

2003 WL 23216812 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMED TAMER H ELSHEHABI
File: A75 985 441 - Newark
October 7, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William F. Henning, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on December 2, 2002. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before March 3, 2003. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until April 17, 2003, but it was rejected for filing defects. Subsequently, the respondent’s motion to reopen was not filed with the Board until May 12, 2003.
 
On June 27, 2003, the respondent’s former attorney of record, Petro Morgos, Esquire, submitted a letter indicating that he never received the Board’s decision in this matter.1 On July 28, 2003, the respondent filed a certification in support of his motion to reopen through his new attorney of record, William F. Henning, Esquire. In his certification, the respondent states that neither he nor his former attorney received the Board’s decision, that he was unaware his appeal had been dismissed when he applied for work authorization. Furthermore, he indicates that he is currently married to a United States citizen and a visa petition has been filed on his behalf. He seeks reopening to apply for adjustment of status.
 
The record reflects that the Board’s decision dated December 2, 2002, was mailed to the respondent’s former attorney at the address provided to the Board on a Form EOIR-27 filed on June 30, 1999: 800 N. Kings Highway, Suite 307, Cherry Hill, New Jersey 08034. The United States Post Office returned the decision to the Board, and the envelope indicates “not deliverable as addressed-unable to forward.” An EOIR-27 subsequently filed with the Board on May 12, 2003, reflected a new address for the respondent’s former attorney: 800 N. Kings Highway, Suite 505, Cherry Hill, New Jerseys, 08034. On June 26, 2003, the respondent’s former attorney filed another Form EOIR-27 reflecting a new address of 6 West Kings Highway, Haddonfield, New Jersey, 08033.
 
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.38(e), attorneys who enter an appearance before the Board have an affirmative duty to keep the Board apprised of their current address and contact information. Changes of address should be made through a Form EOIR-27 (Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Board of Immigration Appeals Executive Office for Immigration Review) within five (5) business days. Thus it appears that on December 21, 2002, the Board’s decision was correctly mailed to the former attorney’s last known address of 800 N. Kings Highway, Suite 307, Cherry Hill, New Jersey 08034, but apparently his former attorney had moved to Suite 505 without notifying the Board. The respondent’s motion to reopen was not filed with the Board until May 12, 2003, and was therefore filed out of time. We cannot make a finding of ineffective assistance of counsel in this instance because the respondent failed to meet the requirements under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: CHIMA DAVID OGUNEWE

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On March 10, 2003, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision. As part of this decision, we considered the respondent’s apparent argument that his counsel’s non-appearance at the removal hearing should be excused. We decided that the Immigration Judge did not err in holding the hearing without counsel, and that in any event the respondent failed to show how the presence of counsel would have affected the proceedings. The respondent filed a motion on April 10, 2003, which is timely if considered a motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899-900, 901-03 (9th Cir. 2003). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent’s attorney, who is the same attorney who failed to appear at the removal hearing, argues that she provided ineffective assistance of counsel by not appearing at the removal hearing, and that the respondent was not able to pursue adjustment of status based on marriage to a United States citizen. The respondent has completely failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Most critically, the respondent must show prejudice resulting from his attorney’s actions. Matter of Assaad, supra, at 556, 561-62; Matter of Lozada, supra, at 638; Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226 (9th Cir. 2002); Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000). As we noted in our earlier decision, the respondent has not submitted an application for adjustment of status or otherwise shown that he is eligible for such relief. The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.

2003 WL 23216850 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NEY ANTONIO DOMINGUEZ A.K.A. RAFAEL JIMENEZ
File: AXX XX7 387 - Napanoch
October 6, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)] - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)] - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated March 4, 2003. In support of the motion to reopen, the respondent states that he received ineffective assistance of counsel as evidenced by the fact that he did not sign an EOIR Form 28, authorizing the law firm of Bretz and Coven to represent him before the Immigration Court. The respondent states that he was unaware that a form had been sent to him for his signature until August 11, 2003. In addition, the respondent argues that the Board improperly dismissed his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to terminate. We will construe this part of the motion as a motion to reconsider. In support of this part of the motion, the respondent essentially repeats arguments made on his behalf in the appeal.
 
To the extent that the instant motion (received on August 27, 2003) requests reconsideration of our prior order in this case, we note that it is untimely. A motion to reconsider must be filed with the Board within 30 days after the mailing of our last decision, or in this case, by April 3, 2003. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). To the extent the instant motion seeks reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel, we note that this application is untimely as well. A motion to reopen must be filed no later than 90 days after the final administrative decision was rendered in the proceedings, or in this case, by June 2, 2003. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Although the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, under whose jurisdiction this case arises, recognizes equitable tolling of such deadlines in ineffective assistance of counsel cases, the court held that an alien must demonstrate not only that his constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000). Here, the respondent gives no explanation for why he let almost 3 months go by before raising the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. It is clear from the record that although the respondent may not have signed an EOIR Form 28, he was well aware that Bretz and Coven was representing him, since he appeared at two hearings while being represented by them. Even if, as the respondent appears to suggest, he was at some disadvantage because his counsel appeared telephonically, it was clear based on comments from the Immigration Judge that attorneys from the Bretz and Coven law firm were representing the respondent’s interests. There is no support for the respondent’s suggestion that he was somehow unaware that these attorneys were acting on his behalf. Finally, we note that the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), which requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. The respondent also has not established that he was prejudiced by any ineffective assistance of counsel, particularly with regard to the telephonic appearance issue. As a matter of fact, in his motion, the respondent repeats key arguments made by his counsel on appeal, thereby contradicting the notion that prior counsel’s representation was ineffective or that it prejudiced his case. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2003 WL 23216813 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA DEL CARMEN BARRERA-DE ZAVALA
File: AXX XX6 918 - Harlingen
October 6, 2003
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa S. Brodyaga, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cheri L. Jones
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(A)] - Excludable at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(7)(B)(i)(II), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(B)(i)(II)] - No valid nonimmigrant visa or border crossing card
  
APPLICATION: Terminate deportation proceedings; remand
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated February 27, 1998, finding her deportable as charged and denying her application for suspension of deportation. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand. The motion will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, last entered the United States on January 2, 1997. The respondent does not contest that, pursuant to section 241 (a)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1251 (a)(1)(A), she was excludable at the time of that entry. Rather, both on appeal and in her motion to remand, the respondent argues that, because she is an immigrant rather than a nonimmigrant, the specific charge of deportability contained in the Order to Show Cause cannot be sustained and, therefore, the deportation proceedings should be terminated. The respondent further argues that her lack of nonimmigrant status is evidence that her former counsel acted improperly in conceding to the charge of deportability. We disagree
 
The record supports, and the respondent does not contest, the finding that the respondent was excludable at the time of entry. Thus, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that, pursuant to section 241(a)(1)(A) of the Act, the respondent is deportable as charged (I.J. at 2).
 
Along with her motion, the respondent has submitted evidence indicating that she has complied with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent, however, has not demonstrated the required prejudice stemming from prior counsel’s actions. See Matter of Lozada, supra at 640. The respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim revolves around her former counsel’s concession to the charge of deportability. The respondent argues that, because of her former counsel’s actions, she was ordered deported on grounds for which she was not deportable. As explained above, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent is deportable pursuant to section 241 (a)(1)(A) of the Act. The respondent does not contest that finding nor has she provided evidence demonstrating that she is not otherwise deportable. Furthermore, the record reveals that the respondent received a full and fair hearing on the merits of her claim before the Immigration Judge. Consequently, the respondent has not shown that she was prejudiced from prior counsel’s conduct and, therefore, we decline to remand the case.
 
*2 In addition, we find that the record supports the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent failed to meet her burden of proving that she acquired the requisite 7-year period of continuous physical presence (I.J. at 8). See section 244(a)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254(a)(1). The respondent was served with the Order to Show Cause on February 17, 1997 (Exh. 1). The respondent failed to provide sufficient testimonial and documentary evidence to prove that she was present in the United States for 7 years prior to that date. As the Immigration Judge noted, the respondent indicated that, in addition to her latest departure and reentry, she has departed and reentered the United States on other occasions since her alleged initial entry (I.J. at 5-8; Tr. at. 55, 81-88; Exh, 6). The respondent, however, was unable to recall and did not provide testimony describing the circumstances surrounding those additional departures and returns (Tr. at 55, 81-88). As a result, the respondent failed to meet her burden of proving the requisite presence.
 
Finally, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent failed to demonstrate the requisite good moral character (I.J. at 8-10). See sections 244(a)(1), (e)(1) of the Act. The DHS submitted both testimonial and documentary evidence refuting the respondent’s testimony denying both that she claimed to be a United States citizen upon her last entry and that she told the inspection agent that she frequently enters this country by claiming United States citizenship (Tr. at 56-57, 78, 166-67, 169-72, 175; Exh. 6). Under the circumstances, it was not unreasonable for the Immigration Judge to conclude that the respondent provided false testimony for the purpose of obtaining immigration benefits and thus failed to demonstrate good moral character.
 
On whole, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s ultimate decision denying the respondent’s application for relief. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LI, ZHAO JIN
File: AXX XX9 584 - New York
October 2, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank R. Liu, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated August 11, 2003. He asserts that, contrary to our finding, his appeal was timely filed and therefore should have been considered. We have reviewed the record and agree with the respondent that his appeal was timely. The motion for reconsideration is granted but the appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent makes three conclusory arguments. He contends first that denial of his motion to reopen by the Immigration Judge was error since he wrote a detailed affidavit. Secondly, he argues that it would have been too “harsh” if he had sued or reported his former attorney to the state bar disciplinary authorities. He was also afraid of antagonizing his former attorney. Thirdly, the respondent asserts that following the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10(1st Cir. 1988), would be “mechanical” and would significantly harm his interests. These allegations form the sole basis for appealing the Immigration Judge’s decision. We affirm the result reached below. An essential element in the respondent’s motion, as the Immigration Judge found, alleged that his failure to show up at his hearing was due, at least in part, to an ineffective assistance of counsel. Nothing on appeal meaningfully addresses the Immigration Judge’s finding that, if so, the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, must be met. The respondent’s arguments on appeal are merely his reasons why he should be exempt from meeting the Lozada requirements. As the respondent has not met his burden in showing that denial of the motion to reopen was clearly erroneous, the appeal is dismissed.

2003 WL 23216892 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CONCEPCION OLIVO-MARTINEZ
File: A78 195 943 - San Antonio
September 30, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nina J. Fantl, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Remand; cancellation of removal; voluntary departure
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM: The respondent is appealing the Immigration Judge’s February 12, 2002, decision finding that his application for cancellation of removal had been abandoned, and denying his request for voluntary departure. On appeal, the respondent argues that he did not file an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), before the Immigration Judge because of the ineffective assistance of his prior counsel and requests that this matter be remanded to the Immigration Judge. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not opposed the respondent’s motion to remand. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Court.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfY the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (lst Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he or she suffered prejudice as a result of the attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640.
 
The record reflects that the respondent has adequately complied with the requirements set forth above. Before proceedings will be reopened, however, it must also be determined that the counsel’s conduct was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA1998).
 
We note from the record that former counsel, Mr. Arnold Garcia, appeared before the immigration Judge on June 11, 2001, and requested an opportunity for the respondent to be considered for cancellation of removal. The Immigration Judge set a deadline of November 1, 2001,for filing the application for relief, and also set a date for a hearing to consider the merits of the respondents application for relief. At this time, the Immigration Judge specifically instructed Mr. Garcia that it was necessary for him to file the application in a timely manner and also necessary that he appear to represent the respondent at the next scheduled hearing, on February 12, 2002, unless he was specifically allowed to withdraw from the case. The application was not completed or filed as required, and counsel did not appear at the continued hearing for which he had notice. On February 12, 2002, the Immigration Judge deemed the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal to be abandoned.
 
*2 In his motion to remand, the respondent avers that former counsel did not assist the respondent in submitting the application for cancellation of removal; failed to inform the respondent of the deadlines associated with the case,1 failed to provide the respondent with his file until all of the deadlines were missed; failed to file a motion to withdraw from his representation of the respondent; failed to inform the respondent concerning a motion to pretermit filed by the Immigration and Naturalization Service (now the Department of Homeland Security,“DHS”); and failed to appear at the final hearing. As a result of this negligence, the respondent’s only opportunity to obtain cancellation of removal was lost.
 
We find that the respondent has established that his failure to file his completed cancellation of removal application by the date required was as a result of ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, supra; Matter of B-B, supra. Moreover, given the evidence before us, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge to provide the respondent a further opportunity to present his application for cancellation of removal and any other relief which for which he may be eligible. The record is remanded for further proceedings and the entry of a new decision.

FOR THE BOARD

The respondent explains that because he does not speak English, he did not understand what was said by the Immigration Judge at the hearing. According to the respondent, the Spanish interpreter did not translate any of the hearing into Spanish, except when the respondent was asked by the Immigration Judge to state his name and to indicate whether he wanted Mr. Garcia to be his attorney.

2003 WL 23216862 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZHONG QING ZHENG
File: AXX XX7 725 - Atlanta
September 30, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reconsider has been filed out of time and will be denied. The Board’s last order in these proceedings was entered on April 21, 2003. A motion to reconsider a decision of the Board must be filed within 30 days after the date of that decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). In the instant case, a motion to reconsider would have been due on or before May 21, 2003. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until May 27, 2003. The motion to reconsider was therefore filed out of time.
 
In his motion, the respondent has raised a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. A motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. In addition, the motion must establish prejudice arising from the alleged failings of prior counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent here has failed to meet these requirements.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.

2003 WL 23216881 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GLORIA JIMENEZ BUSTAMANTE
File: AXX XX1 570 - Los Angeles
September 30, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A) (i)] - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondents’ application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). We reject the respondent’s contention on appeal that all areas of relief were not explained to her and that her due process rights were therefore violated. The respondent in this case was represented by counsel at her last two hearings before the Immigration Judge. See Tr. 19, 26. In addition, a review of the record reflects that prior to the time that the respondent secured counsel, the Immigration Judge questioned the respondent at length in order to determine the forms of relief for which the respondent was eligible. See Tr. 3-8, 12-18. Indeed, we find insufficient evidence to establish that the respondent was provided anything other than a full and fair hearing. Although the respondent maintains that she was not adequately represented by counsel, we find no evidence that the respondent has attempted to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See generally Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir.2000). Moreover, we find no evidence that the respondent was actually prejudiced by the actions of counsel. Id. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*2 NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2003 WL 23216945 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GALINDO HERNANDEZ, FRANCISCO JULIAN (RD)
File: AXX-XX0-792 - LOS ANGELES
September 30, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is untimely. A Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26) must be filed within 30 calendar days of an Immigration Judge’s oral decision or the mailing of a written decision unless the last day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case the appeal must be received no later than the next working day. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.38(b), (c). In the instant case, the Immigration Judge’s decision was mailed on August 26, 1999. The appeal was accordingly due on or before September 27, 1999. The record reflects, however, that the Notice of Appeal was filed with the Board of Immigration Appeals on May 05, 2003. We find that the appeal is untimely. In conjunction with the appeal, the respondent claims that his prior counsel failed to inform him of the Immigration Judge’s decision in his case and failed to inform him of his appeal filing deadline. The respondent has not explained why he waited nearly 4 years after his May 10, 1999, hearing date to file the instant appeal. He has failed to exercise due diligence in pursuing his case. He has failed to meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), and failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel. In fact, the only documentary evidence he has submitted is a nondetailed affidavit and a letter from the State Bar of California stating that his complaint against prior counsel was lacking in detail and could not be acted upon without further information. Furthermore, the respondent has not pointed to any error in fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s 19-page written decision, thus failing to meet the requirement that specific reasons be given for the filing of an appeal. 8 C.F.R. 3.3(b). We do not find this factor to be an adequate reason to accept this appeal by certification. The Immigration Judge’s decision is therefore now final, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, 1003.39, 1240.14, and 1240.15.
 
We note that because we have dismissed the appeal for lack of jurisdiction, either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: If you wish to file a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely, you must file your motion with the Board. However, if you are challenging any other finding or seek to reopen your case, you must file your motion with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). You should also keep in mind that there are strict time and number limits on motions to reconsider and motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2), 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 BIA 1997).

2003 WL 23216772 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION THAT CANNOT BE CITED **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FLORENCIO HUGO M. FULLANTE
File: AXX XX2 393 - Seattle
September 26, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Russell L. Marshak, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Eric Bakken
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)] - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act [8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)] - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; termination
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on January 22, 2003, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen proceedings, alleging ineffective assistance by his former counsel. The motion will be granted and the record will be remanded.
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. In addition, the respondent has submitted a letter from the Washington State Bar Association’s Office of Disciplinary Counsel, indicating that his former counsel has been suspended from the practice of law. Further, we note that the Immigration Judge indicated that the respondent’s former counsel had mishandled multiple immigration matters and that he was the subject of numerous complaints of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Tr. at 14-15. Both the Immigration Judge and the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) noted that the respondent would likely-have a valid claim, on appeal, with regard to ineffective assistance of counsel. Id. We agree.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent indicates that, although he elected to proceed without counsel at his January 4, 1999 hearing, after his counsel failed to appear, he did not realize, as he had not been properly instructed by his former counsel, that he needed to present witnesses and affidavits in order to support his claim. The respondent avers that he was, therefore, prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of his former counsel, in that he was inadequately prepared to state his case and he was unaware of the evidence that he needed to produce in order to establish that his marriage to Felicia was not fraudulent. We find that the record reflects that, indeed, the respondent was not prepared and did not seem to be aware, at his January 4, 1999, hearing, that he needed to provide proof of the legitimacy of his marriage, or what that proof would entail. See Tr. at 27. The respondent, in an affidavit asserts that his former counsel never discussed the need for evidence with him. Considering these assertions, and the respondent’s demonstrated confusion at his hearing, and his compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, we find that the respondent has established that his former counsel’s assistance was ineffective and that it did result in prejudice to him. See generally Agyeman v. INS, 296 F.3d 871, 877 (9th Cir. 2002) (noting that an alien who has not had an adequate opportunity to present evidence is prejudiced); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556 (BIA 2003)(discussing prejudice in the context of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
*2 We additionally note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in which jurisdiction the instant case arises, has held that an Immigration Judge must apprise a respondent, whose marriage is in question, of reasonable means of proving the bona fides of such marriage. See Agyeman v. INS, supra, at 882 (finding that a full and fair hearing can not take place unless a pro se alien is apprised of the evidence that he may present in support of the bona fides of his marriage). Evidence of the marriage’s bona fides may include:jointly-filed tax returns; shared bank accounts or credit cards; insurance policies covering both spouses; property leases or mortgages in both names; documents reflecting joint ownership of a car or other property; medical records showing the other spouse as the person to contact; telephone bills showing frequent communication between the spouses; and testimony or other evidence regarding the couple’s courtship, wedding ceremony, honeymoon, correspondences, and shared experiences. Id. Although it appears that such evidence may be scarce in this case, it is also evident from the record that the need for such evidence was not explained to the respondent, by the Immigration Judge, prior to the January 4, 1999, hearing. See Tr. at 12, 27 (although the Immigration Judge indicates that he previously told the respondent that Felicia’s testimony was needed, we note that the record merely indicates that the respondent’s former attorney was told that her testimony would be very helpful). Thus, the respondent was not given an adequate explanation regarding evidence that could be presented, nor an opportunity to present such evidence.
 
Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings, consistent with the foregoing opinion, and for the entry of a new decision.

2003 WL 25837526 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PRINCE KENNY AGBAI
File: A095 123 464 - Atlanta, GA
May 19, 2003
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; rescission
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on October 3, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind an order of removal entered in absentia on May 12, 2005. Also on October 3, 2008, the Board dismissed the respondent’s motion to reopen the proceedings for adjustment of status. On April 22, 2009, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen and rescind to enable him to apply for adjustment of status, The respondent claims that neither the Immigration Court nor his former attorney, Elihu S. Massel, Esquire, informed him of his duty to appear at the scheduled hearing, and he raises a deficient performance of counsel claim against Attorney Massel. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009) (hereinafter “Matter of Compean”). He further raises a deficient performance of counsel claim against his former attorney Joseph F. DeFelice, Esquire, who assisted him with the filing of his motion to reopen and rescind before the Immigration Judge and his appeal before the Board, because Attorney DeFelice did not raise a viable ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Attorney Massel in compliance with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Id. at 741-42. The motion will be denied.
 
The instant motion is subject to the time and number restrictions imposed on such motions to reopen under sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(iii). Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act, however, an order entered in absentia may be rescinded at any time upon a motion to reopen if the alien demonstrates that he did not receive proper notice of the hearing.¿FN1º
 
Initially, we find that the Notice of Hearing was properly mailed to the respondent at his address of record on September 29, 2004. See section 239(a)(2)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(2)(A). The fact that the respondent engaged the services of Attorney Massel to file a motion to change the venue of the May 12, 2005, hearing, and that the motion to change venue was filed on January 5, 2005, reveals that the respondent was aware of the scheduled hearing. We also find the respondent’s claim that the Immigration Court failed to notify him of his duty to appear to be completely without merit. The Notice to Appear, a copy of which is included in the record, informs the respondent of the time and place of the scheduled hearing, as well as the consequences of the failure to appear. Furthermore, we reject the respondent’s claim that his failure to appear should be excused because Attorney Massel did not inform him of his duty to appear before the Immigration Judge. We emphasize that upon receipt of the Notice of Hearing, the respondent was properly notified of the May 12, 2005, hearing, and of the consequences of the failure to appear. The respondent has not claimed, and there is no evidence to suggest, that Attorney Massel instructed the respondent not to attend the hearing. Finally, the fact that the respondent filed a motion to change venue did not excuse him from his duty to appear, even if Attorney Massel informed the respondent on the eve of the hearing that the motion had not been granted. It is well established that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the alien of his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. See Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985); see also Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988) (holding that the grant or denial of a change of venue motion is within the discretion of the Immigration Judge).
 
*2 In sum, we find that the respondent has failed to establish that Attorney Massel’s representation with regard to the motion to change venue caused the respondent’s failure to appear at the scheduled hearing, thus prejudicing the outcome of this case. As we find that no error on the part of Attorney Massel has been shown, we reject the respondent’s deficient performance of counsel claims against Attorney Massel and Attorney DeFelice. See Matter of Compean, supra, at 732 (an alien who seeks to reopen his removal proceedings based on deficient performance of counsel must, inter alia, establish that his lawyer’s failings were egregious and that he suffered prejudice from the lawyer’s errors). In conclusion, we find that the respondent received proper notice of the scheduled removal hearing, and that he failed to appear for the hearing at the appointed time and venue. Accordingly, the following order denying the respondent’s untimely, number-barred motion to reopen will be entered.¿FN2º
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit (circuit court), in whose jurisdiction this case arises, has determined that no exception can be made to section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act, which provides for rescission within 180 days of the entry of an in absentia order of removal based on exceptional circumstances, e.g., an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273 (11th Cir. 1999).

2

As proper notice has been established in this case, and as the circuit court has determined that no exception can be made to section 240(b)(5)(C)(1)(i) of the Act, we will not further consider the respondent’s arguments with regard to reopening to enable him to apply for adjustment of status. See Anin v. Reno, supra; see also Matter of MS-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998).
